ATKINSON IS PLAINLY
ACCUSED OF TREASON
WASHINGTON, May 2. â The
Postmaster General has direct
ed the Postmaster at San Fran
cisco to take out of the mails for
Manila three pamphlets issued by
Edward Atkinson of Boston, vice
president of the Anti-Imperialist
League. This order does not apply to
the circulation of the pamphlets by
mail in this country, but bars their
dispatch from this country to the
Philippines, discontent and even mu
tiny among the soldiers being stated
by the department to be the design
of those publications.
The three pamplets are specifically
described and under no circumstance
are they to be forwarded by mail to
the Philippines.
The three pamphlets are those which
havo the tit!": "Criminal Aggression
by Whom?" "The Cost *">f a National
Crime" and "Tho Hell of War and Its
Penalties.!'
These, unless something should de
velop to necessitate further action, may
be circulated through the iris within
this country. Accordine to Mr. Atkin
son's own statement, as reported, a
large number of the pamphlets have
beep sent out. The matter was brought
to the attention of the postal officials
by the War Department, whose au
thorities were greatly exercised at a
suggestion that some action be taken.
It is thought there is little possibility
of any of these publications getting
past the San Francisco olfiee. but if a
few should the military authorities in
the Philippines will promptly suppress
them. Postmaster General Smith made
this statement to-day:
'Thes.- pamphlets actually incite to
mutiny, atid it would be utterly un
justifiable i" permit their circulation
among ihe soldiers in I 'â¢.â  Philippines.
Their circulation is a movement to in
duce the soldiers to disobey orders and
in effect to embarrass and resist the
Government in whose service they are
engaged. Their circulation except in
tho mails for the Philippines is not in
terfered with, because, In being sent to
Maniia. they are destined for soldiers
fighting our battles, but in this coun
try the effort to incite to mutiny could
have no result. Not only are they de
signed to incite to mutiny the American
soldiers <*n those islands, but also to
foment and encourage Insurrection on
the part of the Filipinos themselves.
"Such seditious literature as now es
topped might havo an effect on the
forces we now are dealing with that
â would incalculably revert to their ad
vantage, and certainly would tend to
stimulate and strengthen their already
â weakening cause. The law covering the
case is ample. What action might be
takenâ and the offense is of the gravest
characterâ is not to be discussed. I
do not believe there is any Intention to
prosecute Mr. Atkinson, as the matter
now stands. The authority to do so,
however, is plain. He not only attacks
the President and the Government In
the most virulent language ami dis
putes the national policy, but also calls
>-n the American troops to defy the
Government. Certainly this Govern
ment is empowered to stop or check, as
it sees fit, the circulation of these se
ditious publications."
ATKINSON'S PAMPHLETS
OFFICIAL DOCUMENTS
BOSTON, May 2.â Hon. Edwin At
kinson, vice president of the Anti-Im
perialist League of this city, said he
had received no direct communication
from Washington about the action of
the Postmaster General. He explained
that the documents were compilations
of facts and figures taken from debates
in the National House and Senate, and
calling attention to points overlooked
in debate.
"By the action of tho Senate." Mr.
Atkinson continued, "these compila
tions were published as Senate docu
ments, and they are now public docu
ments of the United States. Therefore
any action taken toward keeping them
out of the mails would imply that
Congressional records and official
documents of the United States are un
suitable to be sent til officers "f the
volunteer regiments now at Manila
whose terms of service have expired
and whose return to this country is de
manded by officials of certain States,
who say that the men are entitled to
discharge.
"It is said the dispatch of these
pamphlets from this country to the
Philippine islands is barred lest they
should create discontent and even mu
tiny among the soldiers, that being
stated by the department to be the de
sign of these publications. There are
two pamphlets only. The first edition
of the first, printed in November, was
dedicated to President McKinley In
support of his statement that 'forcible
action would be criminal aggression."
"These pamphlets were, on April 24
and 25, addressed by me to Admiral
George Dewey, President Schurman,
Professor Worcester, General H. G.
Otis. General Lawton, General Miller
and the correspondent of a New York
illustrated weekly in Manila. None of
these have to my knowledge been sent
to any one else. I desired to Bend them
to other officers, and for that purpose
notified the War Department of my In
tention and asked for a list that would
give nic the names. If it is unlawful
for a citizen of th.- United States in
communicate with other citizens in
Manila by sending documents in a pri
vate edition which have been printed
by order of the United States Senate as
public documents. I am content to leavf
the matter at that exact point, which
requires no comment from me.
"1 would, however, call the atten
tion of the Postmaster General to the
necessity of requiring that all news
papers lately printed here and else
where which contains the late speech of
Senator Hoar, given at the Republican
Club in Boston, in which he used this
expressive phrase. 'I can see no differ
ence in 'In' lynching of a Southern
postmaster and lynching a people be
cause they think a government derives
its just powers from the consent of the
governed, and got those Ideas from the
constitution of the United States.' be
taken from the mails.
"If this attempt to forbid free speech
and free mails to the people of this
country has been made, which I can
not believe, I think the people will de
-1 cide themselves what to do about it."

Victims of the Depraved Youth's
Ra<?re Wounded by a Charge
of Bixdshot.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SALINAS, May 2.â Another of those
cases wherein a young boy utterly de
praved either by natural tendencies or
by social contact tries to vent his ani
mosity toward companions came to
light in this city to-day. A lad only
fourteen years old deliberately at
tempted to murder three younger play
mates by discharging the contents of
a shotgun at them, because they re
fused to humor him in some childish
fancy. The would-be murderer has
been arrested.
Louie Ragio. the fourteen-year-old i
son of a prominent Spanish family of j
Santa Rita, a little village several miles
north of this city, was playing oh Sun
day with several companions, among
whom were David Prader, Elmer
Thomas and Pearl Thomas. The latter
three had been forbidden to associate
with young Ragio, who was their elder
by several years, for the reason that it
was generally claimed in the com
munity that his Influence over the other
boys was demoralizing. Ragio insisted
on being allowed to join them. The
boys objected to his presence, giving :
the reason why he was not desired.
Ragio rushed off to his home, not far
away, and soon returned with a shot
gun. Coming within a short distance
Of the lads he made some insulting re
marks and then deliberately raised the
gun and fired at them. Fortunately for
the lads the gun was loaded with bird
shot. The small shot took effect about
the face and body of each. Ragio was
not the least nervous or repentant over
the result of his act, but calmly told
the boys he would teach them a les
son, and i_.\th**t shot was not".' enough
he would return in short order with a
charge of buckshot and "fix them."
The three wounded boys were taken I
to their homes. The would-be murderer j
boastfully told of how ;he tried to kill
his companions, and only to-day, it is â 
asserted, changed his story by saying I
it was an accident. The little lads
stoutly deny this statement and assert j
that Ragio tried to murder them. The j
affair created considerable excitement
in Santa Rita, the sympathizers of
Ragio trying to have the affair hushed, j
while friends of the wounded boys were !
as desirous that the culprit should bo I
punished.
The District Attorney placed a charge
against Ragio to-day and the prelim
inary hearing will come up on Satur
day. '
Will Test the Fee Law.
SAN ANDREAS, May 2.â The County
Board of. Supervisors to-day decided to
make a test case of the law allowing fees
to township Justices. The Auditor was |
directed to refuse to issue a warrant for
th*- amount of one Justice of Peace bill '
which was purposely allowed by the
board. From this refusal it will be taken
to the courts, which will determine
whether the bills shall be paid according
to the law of '93 or '95.

MERRITT SHOWERS
PRAISE UPON THE
SECRETARY OF WAR
DETROIT, May 2.â The Michigan
Society, Sons of the American
Revolution, entertained the vis
iting delegates, officials of the
order and distinguished guests
to-night by a dinner. A feature
which was of peculiar interest was a
speech of General Wesley Merritt.
warmly defending Secretary Alger. He
declared Alger the best Secretary of
War the world has ever seen.
The sneakers chose their own sub
jects, there being no- set responses to set
topics.
Secretary Alger began his speech with
a reference to the struggles of the Pil
grim Fathers to secure "equal rights
and religious freedom to all." and al
luded to the heroic deeds of the revolu
tionary patriots, ancestors of the so
ciety which furnished to-night's enter
tainment-, but he soon turned the at
tention of his' auditors to the more re
cent war with Spain. , The Secretary
spoke at some length of the trials of his
department in equipping the great
army so hurriedly, and expressed his
gratification that ultimately "battles
' were fought and victories won, without
the loss of a gun or a color."
Continuing, he said:
Supposing they had a little beef spoiled,
I what of it? If they had had no beef at
i all they would have had more than Gen
| eral Joe Wheeler and I had In the other
j war. ?â¢â¢..
The people of the United States are an
unselfish people. They have never sought
territorial accession except to the benefit
:of the peoples that came with it. The
flag of this nation has been hoisted over
Cuba. Porto Rico and the Philippines, in
j lands whose people have for centuries
! been oppressed by Spanish misrule.
.Whatever else may be, we can be assured
that as it has been raised in Porto Rico
and. -the Philippines there it will remain. !
j One of these islands we hold in trust; the I
others 'are ours. â¢ The strife that brought |
j upon this country the war with Spain I
was not of our own seeking; the for*.- of
events and the cry of downtrodden hu
manity called for < our aid. No human
mind could stop it. It was to be.
There is not a heart in the breast of any
, true American to-night whose pulsations
do not quicken " with pride over the
achievements of our army and navy. The
i volunteers and the' regulars in the Philip
; pines have won for themselves the na
tion's crown. . All honor to them, and I
I hope and trust that ('ongressional action
shall give such recognition of their serv
ices as shall show to them and to the
world our appreciation of their valor. The
nation mourns for the families who will
never again hoar the footfalls across the
threshold of those they loved; but that is
.the sacrifice they offered, the sacrifice
they have made. .
. We are to-day, we hope, near Ing the
end of the conflict and Hearing the dawn
of peace in the Philippines, and upon
every lip is dally, yes, hourly, expressed:
"What of the, future?" God in his infinite
wisdom can" only answer that question,
and he has guided us thus far, and as his
hand has been. so. plainly. seen in the his
tory of tin 1 'past twelve months we can
feel assured that as he rules, what is
right will be.
; Major General Merritt was the next
speaker. , Said he: *
. When we went to the Philippines there
was, only one. course for us to pursue, viz..
the course that has been taken up to the
present Our navy had captured Manila
Bay and the army had to capture the city
and we could not let go; and the prospects j
are I now that, the American flag will con- ;
tinue to wave in the Far East.
The army in the East, as I testified I
before the Investigating commission in
Washington, had everything the War De
partment could furnish. We lived well
and we did our duty, and we have no
apologies to make or recriminations to
offer. This most outrageously abused ad
ministration has done more for the army
than any other administration that ever
existed. If there were canned speeches j
here to-night they would be better than
some of the refrigerated speeches that we
have read. If I had a canned speech to
deliver, on this subject I assure you it
would not smell.
General Merritt referred to the Secre- 'â 
tary of War as the best Secretary of
War the world has ever seen. He |
added :
No Secretary, was ever more abused. |
Secretary Stanton stood through his
abuse as the most fearless Secretary that
ever lived. We have now another Secre
tary, who has made fewer mistakes and
been more abused than any of ids prede
cessors. He has the confidence of all ex
cept a few malcontents. Through him j
has been attacked the President of the
United States.* He is almost sick at heart ;
at the weight of unjust obloquy that has j
been heaped upon him; but with no prom- j
ises to make or platitudes to indulge in, 1
he is conducting aji honest administration. i
1 was born a Democrat. I don't think j
I'll ever get over it. I'm proud to belong j
to that .political faith. (A voice: "You're j
a Republican, but don't know it.") But |
I hope the time will come when there will |
be no differences between Democrats and j
Republicans, at least manifested against
those who rule the Government. I talk '
as a soldier. I don't commit myself to !
any complications that may lead to
trouble with Germany, but I hug myself
for being an American citizen. j

Hrs. Virgine Hassell Mur
ders Mrs. Wm. Hickman
and Commits Suicide

STOCKTON, May 2.â Mrs. Virgine
Hassell, a dashing young English
woman, murdered Mrs. William
Hickman early this morning and
then ended her own life.
Mrs. Massed was known as "The
Countess." and Mrs. Hickman is said to
have been a victim of her unnatural af
fection. They were found dead in bed.
locked in each other's arms. The re
sponsibility, for the tragedy was easily
fixed by the circumstances surrounding
it. The woman had a. steady hand and
knew where to shoot to produce in
stant death.' Mrs. Hickman was shot
through the. left temple, and the mur
deress then ended her own life with a
bullet through her. right temple.
The scene of the double murder was
the Leffer roomine-house, 216 South
Sutter street, at which, place Mrs. Has
sell made, her home. The intimacy be
tween Mrs. Hickman and Mrs. Hassell
had been a source of comment in cer
tain circles for some time.
Mrs. Hickman w.s a woman with two
children, a daughter of T. J. Smith and
related through marriage to one of the
leading families of Stockton. Of late
Mrs. Hickman's husband and relatives
have been making strenuous efforts to
keep the women apart. Mrs. Hassell
never lost an opportunity to
hunt her up and get her out to visit at
the rooming house. During the last
week the relatives of Mrs. Hickman
were congratulating themselves that
the pair were becoming less infatuated
with each other.
Yesterday afternoon the two met, and
Mrs. Hassell said she had just received
a remittance from England, and in
tended to start back home this morn
ing. She wanted Mrs. Hickman to
spend her last night in Stockton with
her. She also went to the Smith home
and made the same statement, with the
result that the two started for the
private lodging house. The English
woman was seen to- mail some letters at
about 11 o'clock, and also purchased
some bananas at a stand at the same
time.
None of the inmates of the place,
other than "Jack" Lawson, remember
having heard the shots when the
tragedy was enacted. Lawson says
that at about 4:30 o'clock he heard
two reports, a few secconds apart, but
supposed the noise was on the street
and was a part of the Dewey celebra
tion.
The discovery of the crime was due
to the uneasiness of Mrs. Hickman's
father over her having been away all
night with Mrs. Hassell. When the
daughter did not come to the store he
set out for the Hassell lodgings to in
vestigate. He knocked on the door
and received no answer. The father
lost no time in breaking the door open
and the sight terrified him.
The bed was in the northwest corner
of the room. Mrs. Hickman was on
the side next to the wall, lying over
the side, with her head turned toward
Mrs. Hassell. Mrs. Hassell's left arm
was under Mrs. Hickman's head,
caressingly, and Mrs. Hickman's left
hand was resting on Mrs. Hassell's
bosom.
Mrs. Hassell's head was thrown back
and her mouth was open. She was ly
ing on her back, her right arm thrown
across, near the face of her companion,
and the pistol with which the double
tragedy had been committed had ap
parently just slipped from her lifeless
fingers. The hair of the two women
was matted with clotted blood, and the
pillows on which they lay were satur
ated. The doors and windows were all
securely locked, showing that no one2
had entered the apartments. On a
large trunk was a note:
"This trunk and machine, with con
tents, is the property of Miss Edith
Cox, 1723 Valdez street, Oakland."
This was the only message left Mrs.
Hassell had torn up several pictures
of herself, but a large panel portrait of
her friend reposed on the bureau.
Edith Cox, the girl mentioned in the
note, arrived here to-night. She was
Mrs. Hassell's, or as she always re
spectfully addressed her, "the Coun
tess' " maid. She knew the woman in
England. After Virgine Mathilde
Reike, "the Countess," had become the
wife of a remittance man, she sent for
the girl to come out and live with her.
Miss Cox says Mrs. Hassell was
reared In Carlisle Mansion. London, at
which place her mother died less than
eighteen months ago. The mother had
a bona fide title, derived from the
French side of the family, and the
father, who died many years ago. left
considerable property.
"Mrs. Hassell's mother kept up a pa
latial establishment," she continued,
"and when she died, about all she left
to poor Mrs. Hassell was the French
titleâ genuine, I know, but T cannot at
this minute recall it. The young
Countess has had every advantage. She
had her swimming and dancing mas
ters. She rode like a man and often
went off into Scotland on a hunt. She
was finely developed and few men could
equal her for all-around athletics. She
was always odd and did not care a snap
what the people thought of her. She
would go up into her room and smoke a
cigarette and gossip. She always car
ried a small revolver in her bosom ever
since I knew her. She was a dead shot
and brought to this country with her a
gun which she had had made at a cost
of .Â£6O.
"A year ago last January, and shortly
after her mother's death, she got what
money she could together, and, with an
old lady, started out on a trip of the
world. On an ocean liner she met Has
sell, and as he was suave and made
gjpat representations regarding his
landed interests in California, she came
overland to San Francisco with him and
they were married. They came to
Stockton, and in June, at her request,
I came out from England, bringing sev
eral of her trunks with me. I found
them living in a scantily furnished
rented house, and the Countess selling
her silver plate and pawning her valu
ables in order to get food. He put on
airs, and the poor woman even picked
chickens for the market. It ended by
him beating and abusing her, and last
July they separated.
"She went out to' work in a private
family, and later got into a store. She
got some money from England occa
sionally, and I understood she had some
interests remaining there which would
be quite valuable if they ever got out
of court. I went to Oakland to work,
and. like a fool, have been sending
every cent of my earnings to her in
order to Indulge her as far as possible
In what she had been used to.
"A month ago her letters began com
ing irregularly, so I came up to Stock
ton tn see what was the matter. We
ate and slept together, and she seemed
as cheerful as usual. We made plans
to go back to England soon, and she
never once rationed having any new
trouble. She was not a woman ho
would allow any man to break her

though.

giving nearly everything she had in
the way of clothing and fancy articles
to Mrs. Hickman. Her valuables she
had been selling to keep up her wants.
She had several trunks of the finest

all is gone.
"She probably wrote me a letter, but

pet here. That may throw some light

"Mrs. Hassell was born in Rio
Janeiro and was not yet 28 years old.
Her father was, I believe', in the Brit
ish Consular Service. sic- has seen
.^ll kinds of life, from London drawing

Mis. Hickman was known before her

tures.
Has comes of a good English fam
ily. He will bury his wife, whom he

Mrs. Hickman was probably shot
while she slept, as her eyes were closed
as if in sleep. The English woman's
eyes were open and there was a set
look of pain on her face. As far as as
certained to-day Mrs. Hassell had only
75 cents in her possession. Her story
that she had received money from Eng
land and was on the eve of returning
to that country is believed to have been
a myth invented for the deliberate pur
pose of getting Mrs. Hickman into her
power that she might kill her. In the

father of Mrs. Hickman, said:
"One day she wanted Emma to go
riding with her and I refused to allow
it. Mrs. Hassell made some remark to
the effect that she would rather die
than be separated from Emma. I then
became afraid of the woman."

CLERGYMEN MEET
AT PACIFIC GROVE
Episcopal Convocation Opens Its Ses
sions in the Seaside
City.
PACIFIC GROVE, May 2.-One of the
most Interesting conventions this town of
many conventions has ever known opened
at St. Marys by the Sea, the pretty little
Episcopal church of this city, this after
noon. It is the convocation of San Jose
of the Episcopal church, and numbers
among its members some of California's
leading clergymen. The ministerial visit
ers were net at th*' train by a reception
committee and driven to the parish house,
where luncheon was served. Then they
proceeded to the church.
Rev. 17 B. A. Lewis of Gilroy, dean of
this convocation, called the body to order.
He presented a report of the work of his
diocese, after which Dr. L. Delos Mans
field of San Jose, treasurer of the convo
cation, made a financial statement. Rev.
Dr. Gardner, the popular young Palo Alto
pastor, was appointed essayist for the
next convocation, his topic to be "The
Relation of Science and Religion."
The object of the convocation is the con
sideration of missionary work to be found
in this diocese and the solution of various
problems affecting the growth of the
church. The afternoon was therefore
chiefly devoted to a discussion of "Chris
tian Science," so called. Rev. J. A.
O'Meara of Santa Clara opened with a
paper ln which the subject was looked
! at from three points of viewâ theological,
scientific and therapeutic. He declared
"Christian science" was a misnomer, the
i cult it designated being essentially not
! scientific, since science classified prov
: able knowledge and was exact and not
j Christian, for it regarded Jesus as a man
of human parents and not as the divine
Christ, thus striking away the very foun
dation of Christianity. ".';-''
"Christian science," he said, "cannot
| stand the test of common-sense. It denies
| the existence of matter and the trust
worthiness of the evidences of the senses,
I while the greatest scientists teach that
matter does â¢â  exist and that the senses
are trustworthy."
Looking at the subject with relation to
therapeutics, he said Christian - science
had been successful in some cases, operat
ing on the principle of the domination of
mind over matter, where the trouble was
of an imaginary nature; but that, ft was
baffled when confronted with surgery.
The paper concluded- with, the statement
that Christian science was the enemy of
I the church of God. because it denied some
of the -most cherished doctrines of the
church and denounced them as delusions.
Vespers were celebrated at 7:30 o'clock.
Rev. William Ford Nichols, Bishop of
California, officiating, assisted by Arch
deacon Emery and twelve other clergy
men. After service the Bishop made an
address upon missionary work in the dio-
I cese of California.

GOES TO CHICAGO
CHICAGO. May 2.â A special to the
Times-Herald from Washington says:
General T. M. Anderson has been as
signed to the command of the Depart
ment of the Lakes, with headquarters at
Chicago. General Anderson is at San
Francisco, having returned but recently
from Manila, and he is expected to as
sume his new command within a few
days. He notified the War Department
a day or two ago that he was ready for
further duty, and lt is presumed he will
start immediately for Chicago.
The new commander of the Department
of the Lakes is a gallant soldier, with a
long and distinguished career to his
credit. He is a major general of volun
teers- and a brigadier general ln the regu
lar army. His latest service has been in
tha Philippines.

PASTOR CRABB IS
DENIED A DIVORCE
Must Provide for His |
Wife's Support.
â â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, May 2.â The Rev. W. D.
Crabb, the Methodist divine, has been de
nied a divorce from- his wife, Hattie A.
Crabb. Instead he must pay $20 a month
for her maintenance, besides the cost of
the suit. This was the decision of
Judge Kittredge to-day.
The case, was reviewed by the court.
The charge, on which a divorce was
sought was cruelty, resulting in great
mental suffering. In deciding the case
Judge Kittredge said several things must
be considered. Were the. accusations of
defendant against her husband false? Did
the accusations cause grievous mental In
jury, or did these feelings arise from the
fault of the plaintiff? The accusations
were admitted to be false,, but the court
did not believe the mental suffering of the
pastor arose from the accusation. In
stead there was something else back- of
this trouble. The whole case was a
mystery. He did not believe the true
| cause had been brought out at the trial.
: Hints of the disturbing element could be
i found in the correspondence between Dr.
I Crabb and his wife.
The court said Dr. Crabb's unhappiness
! arose from the fact that he considered
| his wife insane. The attention of the
! minister to teachers while at the Univer
; sity of the Pacific and to his female
: parishioners at Gilroy had nothing to do
with It. He was possessed of the idea
that his wife was Insane seven years ago.
At that time he made the matter known.
Dr. Crabb suffered from this and it
seemed to blight his life. His letters
showed this. That he fully believed his
wife deranged mentally was borne out by
his having sent her to a private asylum at
| Livermore. and later to the Agnews Asy
| lum. During the trial It was conceded
! Mrs. Crabb was sane and responsible, so
i the court would not decide that matter.
| The charge of grievous mental suffering
j through false accusations had not been
I sustained and the divorce was denied.
Judge Klttredge said he did not be
lieve Dr. Crabb had ever written a con
! fession of his love for other women." and
i hinted that the confession produced
In court was forged. He did not believe
there had been any undue intimacy by
Dr. Crabb with female members of his
parish.
It was ordered that the first $20 for
maintenance be paid on May 15, and that
payments be made thereafter on the Ist
jof each month. It was doubtful whether
Mr. Crabb could pay this sum. His sal-
I ary now in a South San Francisco church
! was only $19 a month. Besides this he had
but a few household goods and an inter
est In a parcel of land. Yet he should
contribute something to his wife's sup- j
port. I _
Attorney C. W. Cobb, counsel for Dr.
Crabb. was granted a ten days' stay.

SLIGHT DAMAGE
DONE BY FROST
Fruit Around Fresno Is
Nipped.
â â¦ â -
Special Dispatch to The Call.
FRESNO, May 2.â number of vine
yardists who were seen to-day, â  said
that fully a quarter of the crop was
ruined by the late frost and that the j
! raisin product would be lessened ac- ;
j cordingly. Not since 1896 was such a j
late and so severe a frost experienced. I
Such reports are invariably exaggerat- j
ed, but there is no gainsaying the fact
that the late frost did considerable
damage. A peculiar thing about the
frost is that it seemed to come in cur
rents. Certain vineyards were dam
aged, while adjacent places escaped al- j
j together, or were only slightly bitten.
I The generally accepted theory is that j
the cold spell went in currents, al- j
! though it may be that local conditions |
j in certain vineyards enabled them to
withstand the frost better than ad
jacent places. For the reason that the
damage was not general it cannot
well be expressed in figures. "';'
SONOMA, May From the present |
indications the fruit crop in this vicin
ity has not been seriously damaged by
frosts. At present an abundant crop
j of fruits, with the exception of cherries,
I prunes and pears, is expected. Of. the
| three varieties excepted, a fair crop is
i assured, while apricots will be very ;
! scarce. Apple orchards and most of
! the peaches will need thinning. Vine- ,
j yards are looking fine and an abundant !
crop is expected. The grain and hay
I crops are both excellent. The showers
I of last week were of great benefit to
both.
RED BLUFF, May 2. â Manton.
thirty-five miles northeast of. Red
Bluff, was visited by a severe frost Fri
day morning and the entire fruit crop
was damaged. Even strawberries suf
fered. Manton is the principal apple- |
growing locality in the county and the |
losses necessarily will be very heavy.

Raisin Combine a Success.
FRESNO, May The success of the
, California Raisin Growers' Association is 1
now assured. At a meeting held to-night j
it was decided that the association had '
secured the requisite 90 per cent of the
crop of the five central counties. The
packers accepted the figures and will
work in unison with the growers' associa
tion. The success of the pool, which was
! hovering in the balance, is a matter for
congratulation among the growers.

sireiuan nt sÂ«*uÂ«__ orgau. DtooL '*& Mar____i_,GRAND COURT OF
FORESTERS OPENS
AT SANTA CLARA
SANTA CLAM, May 2.â The eleventh annual session of the Grand Court,
Foresters of America, convened here thla morning. Two hundred and twenty
flve delegates were In attendance. Many delegates have their wives with them
and Santa Clara Is entertaining the visitors In a hospitable manner. The ho
tela re full and many persons have found convenient quarters In private houses.
Ihe streets and business houses are gaylv decorated In holiday attire, and a
brass band discourse.! at intervals during the day and evening.
â  The court convened at 10 o'clock, Grand Chief Ranger J. Samuels presiding.
The usual committees were announced and several resolutions wore Introduced
and referred. A note from the Social Club was received, the delegates being in
vited to use the club rooms during the week. The town officials Invited the del
egates to participate In a parade in the afternoon and attend the exercises In
the public park. This was accepted and the session adjourned until Wednesday
morning.
In the afternoon there was a parade of the delegates, the local court of For
esters and the citizens of the town. At the park Mayor Druffel made a speech
ot welcome and bade the visitors enjoy the freedom of the town. Grand Chief
Rancor Samuels responded. The rest of the afternoon was spent by many of the
delegates in driving about the country.
To-morrow will be a day of interest. Important questions will be brought
up for discussion. The principal one is that of holding biennial sessions in the
future. It will provoke a hot debate and a close vote is expected,
To-morrow afternoon the election of grand officers will occur. A good deal
cf political work is being done in this connection. Candidates are numerous and
many combinations are reported, but good feeling prevails. Those who are "on
the inside" claim that the results of the election will be as follows: Grand chief
ranger, J. W. Hughes; grand sub-chief ranger. Hugo K. Asher; grand senior
woodward. John Heenan. Southern California will name the grand junior wood
ward, and the choice lies between J. F. Greenough, W. A. Weldon and R. A.
Douglas. Senior beadle. Charles H. Hogg; junior beadle, Lee Wells; trusteesâ
E. Lampe. Dr. George Pache and one other to be chosen from a half dozen as
pirants.
The positions of grand representatives are the ones sought. Thirteen are to
he chosen, and there are thirty candidates. Those most frequently mentioned
are: A. Orflla, H. c. Warner, C. P. Rendon, J. Samuels. C. A. Bolderman, J. F.
Severance, S. A. White, Sylvester Shaben, Charles Ing, R. R. Dunstan. E. S.
Wolf. H. Krymmtnger, C. A. Melvill, .1. S. W. Saunders and R. Rasmussen.
On Wednesday night Senator Wolfe will lecture on "The Good of the Order."
Afterward there will be a social dance. On Thursday night there will be ,1
banquet and on Friday night a ball will be given at the Rose Carnival Pavilion
on the alaxneda, half way between here and San Jose. >*--
To-night the hilarious side degrees were administered, and initiations were
numerous.

MATAAFA' MEN
STILL DEFIANT
Situation in the Samoan
Islands Very Critical.
Special Dispatch to The Call,
WELLINGTON. J*. Z.. May Advices
from Samoa indicate that the instructions
received from the powers to cease hos
tilities make the situation in the islands
very serious, as the rebels hold the main
food supplies and are again near the
municipality of Apia.
The correspondent of Reuters News
Agency thinks it Is evident thai the home
authorities have misunderstood the situa
tion in Samoa. The Germans and rebels
are already jubilant and claim a victory
over the British and American forces.
The correspondent says that unless the
rebels are made to submit the difficulties
will remain unsolved. The white people
in the outskirts of the city are at the
mercy of the rebels.
The American Vice-Consul's store at
Falifa was looted last week, while a Ger
man store adjoining it was left untouch
ed, British and American residents all
strongly condemn the action of the pow
ers In ordering cessation of hostilities
at this stage.
Two days after the last battle at Vaili
ma Lieutenant R. A. Gaunt of the Brit- \
ish cruiser Porpoise marched his brigade
Into Valllma unopposed and was followed
by a company or British bluejackets un
der Captain Sturdee and Lieutenant
Cave. An inspection pf the battlefield
shows that the defenses erected by the
rebels near the home of the late Robert
Louis Stevenson were strong and well
planned. The loss sustained by the reb
els, it is evident, was much greater than
was at first supposed. The main section
of Mataafa's followers, who were en
gaged in the battle of Valllma, retreated
westward to Lulumoenga, where they lay
In wait for frlendlies. Orders had been
given them to capture Lieutenant
Gaunt's head. The rebels were also anx
ious to secure the superior weapons with
which his brigade ls armed.
It is rumored that thirteen boats, with
armed rebels, are approaching from the
east. The town appears to lie safe.

TO SECURE A NEW TRIAL
The Court of Cassation Said to Be
About Evenly Divided on the
Question.
LONDON, May 3.â The Paris corre

been ascertained that the court is so even
ly divided that probably there will be un
der the least favorable conditions only a
majority of two against Dreyfus revision;
but as it Is certain that there will be ab
sences owing to Illness and other causes
â  the final decision will be swayed by Presi
â¢ dent Ballot de Beaupre. who is believed
i to favor a retrial by court-martial."
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MAY 3, 1899.
THE FILIPINO ARMY MUST
SURRENDER UNCONDITIONALLY
MANILA, May The second
conference, held this morning
between General Otis and the
Filipino emissaries, Colonel
Manuel Arguelles and Lieu
tenant Jose Bernal, terminated with
out any definite results.
MANILA, ': May 2.â Major Manuel ,
Arguelles and Lieutenant Jose Bernal j
of the staff of General Antonio Luna re- j
'.. turned to-day to renew the request of l
General Luna for a cessation of hostili
ties. They have found the field of \
> labor a most uncongenial one. , The
two officers were received in confer
! ence with Major General Otis. The
proposals which they had to submit
differed but slightly from those which j
they brought from the Filipinio com- I
mander in the flrst place. They de
i sired a little time in which to sum
mon Congress, and expressed ' them
selves at being confident that the Con
gress will decree peace, because the
i people desire it. They represent that
j Aguinaldo is without power to sur- ]
I render the army, and that the Congress
i must decide that question. Incident
! ally, the Filipino envoys asserted that
Aguinaldo had not yet made a fair
test of his strength against the Amer
lean forces, because only one-third of
his army had been assembled to
gether.
The envoys presented a letter from
Mabini, President and Minister of For
! eign Affairs in the Cabinet of Aguin- j
aldo, and who is the backbone of the |
, Filipino organization. The communi- j
cation is personal and unofficial . and j
seconds Major Arguelles' arguments.
Whether or not General Luna is sin- j
I ' cere in his overtures, the negotiations
: are giving the insurgents a much-need
ed opportunity to rehabilitate their de
moralized force The truth is that dis
sensions among the leaders of the ;
rebels is helping on the disorganization j
of the Filipino army almost as much as I
the American campaign.
In the course of the conference with
the Filipino envoys, General Otis agreed
with the statement of Major Ar
guelles that the people of the Philippines
wanted peace, and he added that re
quests for protection are pouring In :
upon him from all parts of the country.
Major Genera] Lawtc-n was engaged;
in hard fighting early this morning. ;
Telegraphic communication was cut at 9
a. m., and there have been no reports
from Gent Lawton since that time.
The first authentic information re
garding Lieutenant J. C. Gillmore and
his oarty of fourteen men from the
I'nited States gunboat Yorktown. who
were captured by the Filipinos on April
12, was received to-day atÂ» the hands of
Major Arguelles. It is in the form of a
list of the missing men, and is signed
by Lieutenant Gillmore. The Lieutenant
reports that he and his party have been
brought across the mountains from
Baler, where they were captured.
This Information was brought in re
sponse to a note which Major General
Mac Arthur sent to Luna by Major
Shields and Lieutenant Hayne, and
which these officers, bearing a flag of
truce, carried across General Luna's
line yesterday evening.
WASHINGTON, May 2.â The follow
ing cablegram was received at the War
Department at midnight:
MANILA. May 3.â Adjutant General,
Washington: List of prisoners in the
hands of Insurgents just received shows
Lieutenant Gillmore and seven enlisted
men in the navy lost from the Yorktown,
and six enlisted men from the army.
Three of the six were wrongfully arrested
in January before hostilities commenced.
All reported to be doing well. Besides the
above there are two men in the hands of
the Insurgents of the south. Captain
Rockefeller is still unaccounted for.

COLONEL FUNSTON MADE
A BRIGADIER GENERAL
WASHINGTON, May 2.â The President
has appointed Colonel Frederick Funston
of the Twentieth Kansas a brigadier gen
eral of volunteers. This appointment was
recommended by Major General Mac A
rthur and supplemented very strongly by
General Otis In a cable dispatch received
yesterday. General Mac Arthur said the
services of Funston during the campaign,
especially In the crossing of the Rio
Grande, deserve recognition such as his
promotion to be a brigadier general, also
that as a leader of troops Colonel Fun
ston was particularly valuable. The Presi
dent recognises the services of Colonel
Funston. and his first official act on return
ing to Washington was to make him a
brigadier general. The cable General Otis
sent yesterday says: "Mac Arthur strong
ly recommends Colonel Funston's ap
pointment as brigadier general for signal
skill and gallantry In crossing the Rio
Grande and most gallant services since
the commencement of the war.- I urge
his appointment. Funston Is an able lead
er of men and has earned recognition."
AGUINALDO DENIES THAT
HE IS SUING FOR PEACE
LONDON, May 2.â Representatives of
the Filipino Junta, say they have received
a telegram from Aguinaldo, dated April
30, ln which he states that his Govern
ment has nothing to do with the present
peace negotiations, which, he asserts, are
being conducted by a group of half-castes
and Creoles, who are anxious for peace
in the expectation of getting high oflices
under a new government. Several of these
men, Aguinaldo says, are members of the
Filipino Congress, where they are endeav
oring to outvote him. Aguinaldo added
that he had had no direct negotiations
witn the Americans since the fall of
Malolos, when certain proposals passed
between D. .C. Worcester of the American
Philippine Commission and a representa
tive or Aguinaldo to peace based upon In
dependence with an American alliance.
Aguinaldo disavows the present negotia
tions and adds that under no circum
stances will he accept an American pro
tectorate. The dispatch concludes with a
contemptuous reference to Major Argue
les as a Spanish officer who has no more
authority to act on behalf of Aguinaldo
than has Luna himself. â¢ -\yy '--.--
Maher and Buhlin Draw.
NEW YORK, May 2.â Peter Maher and
Gus Ruhlin, "the Akron Giant," fought a
twenty-round draw before the Lenox Ath
letic Club to-night. Maher was nine
pounds the lighter of the two. Maher was
the odds-on favorite at ' 100 to 60. Six
thousand people saw the fight. Maher
weighed 181 pounds and Ruhlin 190. ,

NO WONDER "AGGY"
IS A HOME RULER
NEW YORK, May 2.â Peter Scan lan of Jersey City, whose reputation
for voracity Is excellent, declares Aguinaldo, who is giving the Americans
so much trouble in the Philippines, is the son of an Irishman. Mr. Scanlan
says the Filipino leader's right name is O'Gormelly. His father, he says, was
a native of Tralee, County Kerry, who went to Hongkong, where he married
a wealthy Japanese woman. He then moved to Manila. There he engaged
in the grocery business and made a fortune. Aguinaldo was born in Manila,
and In his youth was sent by ids father to Madrid to be educated for the
priesthood, but the lad objected to that calling, aad told his father he wished
to he a second George Washington and free his people. His father then dis
owned him and made a will cutting him off from any share of his prop
erty. : fff _
Mr. Scanlan thinks it is the Irish blood in Aguinaldo that makes him
such a fighter.
NICKEL MACHINES
WILL HAVE TO GO
Mayor of Los Angeles
on the Warpath.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, May 2.â Mayor Fred
Eaton at to-day's session of the City
Council sent a snappy message to that
body by which he returned the ordi
nance to license the nickel-in-the-slot
machines without his approval. This
ordinance was prepared to assist in
raising additional revenue, owing to the
reduced condition of the city treasury.
Mayor Eaton in his message says that
he objects to the ordinance, as it pro
vides for the licensing ' of machines
which pay winnings in money, and that
they are "a notorious sure-thing me
chanical swindle."
After reciting that the City Attorney
has informed him that the machines
are not made illegal devices by law and
according to the rulings of the Supreme
Court cannot be prohibited, he says he
does not believe the city is reduced to
the necessity of gaining . revenue by
licensing such swindling devices, and
that other games might be taxed which
give the player a more even chance.
The message concluded with a broad
side that startled the city patriarchs:
"It seems somewhat anomalous that
in a city where it is lawful to drink
bottled beer on Sunday but unlawful
to drink the same brand of beer when
drawn from a keg the City Council
should license one of the most vicious
forms of petty gambling that is prac
ticed."
The whole was referred to a com
mittee, which in its report said:
Your committee iK appalled to contem
plate the amount of irreparable damage
that would be" occasioned by such a com
promise with satan; an eternity would
not be of sufficient duration to overcome
the evil Incubated during that period of
the maintenance of devices of the char
acter described by the Mayor.
The measure failed to pass over the
Mayor's veto.
To-night Mayor Eaton said he. would
call a special meeting of the Police
Commission to-morrow for the purpose
of giving saloons notice to remove from
their premises all slot machines paying
money to the players under penalty of
losing their saloon licenses.
"If the Council won't take the mat
ter in hand it gratifies me to be able
to do it," said the Mayor.

SOLDIERS A RE
NOW ON GUARD
Confidence Is Gradually
Restored at Wardner.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WARDNER, Idaho, May 2.â The
presence of soldiers has eased the situ
ation remarkably, and to-night the
people are gradually regaining slight
confidence, but it is felt that peace can
not be assured until 500 troops are
quartered here. More troops will ar
rive to-morrow. Captain Batchelor and
his gallant seventy-six soldiers, as
sisted by 100 townsmen, who are un
der arms, would give such resistance
as would annihilate any ordinary force,
but should the 1300 men up . Canyon
Creek arrive fully armed, the little
guard now here could hardly hope to
cope with them.
The old militia guns to the number of
about seventy have all been distributed
among the men here, and what State
ammunition remained has also been
distributed among the men. About
7000 rounds of ammunition belonging to
the old militia force, and stored in the
vaults at the B.unker Hill office, were
lost in the wreck of that property. Be
sides the State guns all the private
arms in town have been put to use.
One group of seventy men is on guard
at the camp of Captain Batchelor's
soldiers. Besides half as many men
are watching the station. Up at the
Bunker Hill mill is another group of
armed men. To-night they came down
to offer their defense to the town, but
later decided that it would be better
for them to return to the property.
This morning a little bunch' of
Bunker Hill men guarding the tram
way were overtaken by some of Sheriff
Young's deputies and their guns taken
from them. Later, on the command of
Auditor Sinclair, the Sheriff consented
to return the rifles to their owners, and
they have been continuing their watch.
State Auditor Sinclair is working
night and day to bring about the ar
rest of the criminals, and is vigorously
assisted by Coroner France. Twelve
complaints have been made out against
the conspirators, but the work is de
layed by the slowness of Prosecuting
Attorney Samuels. Francis Turner has
been arrested. The officials say that he
has positively been identified as one of
the masked rioters who blew up the
Bunker Hill mill. He was released on
$4000 Vail. â¢â -.;' :-7\-.W'.7.
The inquest to-morrow will be a dou
ble one. The body of James Cheyne,
the Bunker Hill man shot, by the
rioters, who died this afternoon in
Spokane, where he/was taken for hospi
tal treatment, will be brought back here
to-morrow. The inquest will take place
at 2 o'clock in the afternoon at Ward
ner Junction. Attorney General Hayes
is now on the road here to conduct the
prosecution. .:.â¢ .
To-night on the flat by the railroad
station little eampfires are flickering,
and the faint call of a bugle is sounding
"taps" for the night. There Captain
Batchelor and his trusty fighters are
stationed. Near them is a little force
of armed men from the nearby town
ready for any emergency.
J Quite a number of union men left to
day on the trains, going in both direc
tions. But two arrests have been made,
and these are both out on bail. Martial
law has not yet been declared, but it is
expected that it will be to-morrow.
Coroner France will commence the in
quest on . John Smith, the union man
killed last week, as soon aa witnesses j
can be subpenaed. *
ITALIAN CABINET
MAY STEP DOWN
Strong Disapproval of
Government's Policy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ROME, May 2.â ln the Chamber of Dep
uties to-day there was a prolonged and
animated discussion regarding the Gov-,
ernment's foreign policy, growing out of
various interpellations. The debate was
adjourned until' to-morrow, when the
Government will make a statement cov
ering all the questions raised.
In the course of the discussion Admiral
Canavero, Minister for Foreign Affairs,
created a lively sensation by producing,
in response to the attacks upon his policy
at San Mun Bay, China, an official letter
signed by Count *Bonin Longare. Under
Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs, in
the Cabinet of Marquis di Rudini, ad
dressed to the then Minister of Marine
Signor Brin, requesting the latter to ini
tiate some action that would secure for
Italy a station in China. The production
of the letter demonstrated that the lead
ers of the present opposition really ini
tiated the policy they are now oppos
ing.
Admiral Canavero's action in making
public a secret document evoked sharp
comment from Marquis di Rudini. Signor
Crispi, former Premier, and other lead
ing members of th*- Chamber.
Finally General Pellieux, the Premier,
asked that the debate be adjourned, and
the House rose amid considerable excite
ment. The Cabinet will meet to-morrow
morning, and it is rumored that General
Pellieux will tender the resignation of the
Ministry to King Humbert. General Pel
lieux went to the Quirinal this evening
and reported the situation to King Hum
bert. 7V<7-
The scene in the chamber when Signor
Crispi rebuked Admiral Canavero for di
vulging state documents was very dra
matic. "We are giving with light hearts."
said Signor ("rlspi, "a sorry spectacle to
Europe. I ask the Chamber to cease this
discussion." â¢â â ''.
The Deputies treated Signor Crispi with
extraordinary deference, several rising
and exclaiming. "He is our only man."
As. three-fourths of the Deputies are
likely to vote against the Government, it
is believed that General Pellieux has de-
cided to repeat a strategem often employ
ed by Marquis di Rudini and to resign
forthwith, in which case King Humbert
will probably invite him to reconstruct
the Cabinet.
FINED FOR HAVING
STRUCK JAKE RAUER
SAN RAFAEL, May 2.â John Quinn, the
turfman, pleaded guilty to a charge of
simple assault in Justice of the Peace
Rodden's court this morning and was
fined $25. Quinn was charged with hav
ing assaulted Jake Raver. the San Fran
cisco collection agent, on the Tiburon
ferry-boat two weeks ago. The difficulty
between the men arose from an attempt
by Raver to collect a bill due the Bald
win Hotel. It came out at the trial that
the bill was owed by Charles Quinn. a
brother of the defendant, who is at pres
ent in the East.
All the Quinns looked alike to Raver
and he tackled the wrong man In a street
car, demanding payment of the account.
Quinrt was escorting some ladies to the
racetrack at the time, which prevented
him giving full expression to his resent
ment. The next time he met Raver was
on the Tiburon ferry, when he planted his
fist on the collection agent's forehead,
knocking him down.
Raver arrived at the courtroom to-day
just as Quinn was satisfying the fine Im
posed by the court. He was much dis
gruntled that the fine was so low and
heaped anathemas upon the head of the
court and officers, He wanted the trial
to go on, claiming that imprisonment
alone could avenge the punishment he re
ceived from the racing man.

CITIZENS WILL
BE PROTECTED
Honduras and Nicara-
qua to Be Taught
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, May 2â The Herald's
Washington correspondent sends the
following: There is reason to believe
that the administration proposes to use
the Pears case and the Bluefields inci
dents to compel respect for the United
States in Honduras and Nicaragua,
While it is assumed that the Govern
ments of Honduras and Nicaragua
wish to act fairly, their subordinates
have been guilty of petty persecutions
of American citizens which, in many
instances, cannot be made the subject
of official complaint. A letter written
to the State Department last week by
a resident of Honduras shows the
character of the treatment accorded to
Americans in that country, and com
munications from Americans in Nica
ragua indicate that they are subject
ed to all manner of petty annoyances.
The State Department is therefore de
termined to bring the Hondurans and
Nicaraguans to account.
With this object in view the proposi
tion of the Honduras Government that
the Pears case be referred to arbitra
tion has been rejected and Minister
Hunter has been instructed to press
for an immediate settlement.
Should the $10,000 indemnity demand
ed be not forthcoming within a reason
able time a squadron will be ordered
to Honduras to enforce payment.
In the Bluefields matter the authori
ties are determined that General Torres
shall be removed and American citi
zens there shall not pay the double
assessment ordered.
On the part of the Navy Department,
cause of complaint against Nicaragua
arises out of the failure to get two ca
blegrams to Commander Dayton, com
manding the Detroit, now at Bluefields.
Similar trouble was experienced some
months ago in the case of the gunboat
Marietta, and the Detroit was sent to
Bluefields to make an investigation. At
that time President Zeyala denied
there had been any international stop
page of messages, and the matter was
finally dropped. The authorities are
much annoyed at the repetition of the
trouble, and steps will be taken to pre
vents its reoccurrence.
Frank Pears, a United States citizen
of Pittsburg, Pa., was killed by a sentry
at San Pedro Sula during the revolu
tionary uprising cm February 2 last. A
dispatch to the Herald from Washing
ton on April 17 last said that the United
States Government had demanded $10,
--000 indemnity for the heirs of Pears, the
Honduras Government having allowed
his murderer, who was a soldier, to
escape, justice by going into the in
terior. Minister Hunter was instructed
to demand indemnity and the punish
ment of the murderer.
, The Bluefields incident referred to is
the demand of General Torres, Presi
dent Zelaya's representative, that
American merchants should again pay
import duties for which they already
had been taxed by General Reyes, the
recent revolutionary leader, while he
was in power.
Outlaw to Act as Lawyer.
AUSTIN, Tex., May 2.â Charles Beeler,
who is in jail at San Antonio, charged
with the robbing of the Wells-Fargo Ex
press of $60,000 In coin, refuses to employ
an attorney and Bay? he will present his
own case when it comes to trial. He has
asked the court to grant him an immedi
ate trial. He still refuses to divulge the
hiding place of the stolen money.

MINERS RUSHING
TO KRAMER CAMP
Special Dispatch to Thp Call.
RANDSBURG. May 2â Not since the
free milling gold strike by Burcham,
Singleton and Moore at what is now
Randsburg, in April, 1895, has there
been such excitement among miners
on the Mojave desert as the news of
gold strikes ten miles southeast of
Kramer has created in the last week.
Indeed, men who have lived out here
among the bold and bleak mountains
if the painted desert say there has not
been such general excitement because
of new ore rinds since the day.s of the
Calico silver discovery, nineteen ears
ago. After unusual secrecy by th*- dis
coverers of the Kramer mines for four
months, information concerning the
richness of the finds leaked out on
April 22, and forthwith the rush for
Kramer began from all of the little
mining camps from hachapi to the
Colorado River. This week the rush is
greater than ever, and before Sunday
it probably will comprise hundreds of
men. Miners on horseback, on foot, on
the Santa Fe Railroad trains and in
wagons are going into the Kramer
camp by scores daily, notwithstanding
the coldest and windiest May weather
known in years. Last night over 400
men slept under blankets amid grease T
wood chaparral on the desolate foot
hills around Kramer and to-night there
will be more there.
For over a year Albert S. Clarke,
Dixon Duncan and Robert Goldsberry
have been drywashing for gold among
the desert foothills in that locality.
They are old soldiers of fortune and
have had twenty-two years' experience
among the Southern California mines.
Last November they found drywashing
ore near Krai that paid them $30
daily. They agreed to keep their suc
cess a strict secret.
While they conducted their dry
washing they prospected for ledges. In
January they found them. No human
being was within ten miles and they
kept their work secret among them
selves. Meanwhile they have been lo
cating twelve claims on the richest ore
and in localities most advantageous to
themselves; and have sunk shaft: and
crosscuts to reveal the formation of
the ore bed. These miners have been
shipping their drywashed gold to San
Francisco from four different Santa Fe
Railroad stations in order to let no one
know what wealth they were digging.
However, when they sent $2200 worth
of gold from Barstow last week the
secret got out and the rush to Kramer
began.
C. A. Burcham, the millionaire miner
of Randsburg, has mining prospectors
locating claims at Kramer. He says
the Kramer camp will be second to
Randsburg. D. A. Douglas, who has
been a mining expert for the De la Mar
Company of I'tah, says the Kramer
gold strike is the more wonderful be
cause of the peculiarity of its ore. No
such gold ore ever was found in Amer
ica before. It is quartzite, porphyry
and quartz proper. It assays all the
way from $I."* to $600. The vein is. twen
ty feet wide, but In some localities Its
width is over thirty feet. The ledge
may be traced for four or five miles.
W. C. "Woodward, who has charge of
the Randsburg Hallway, has seven
claims there. Shafts on two of them
are down twenty feet and the ore runs
from $6 to $20 a ton from the grass
Boots. 11. R. Bacon, who was first on
the camp last week, has seen Dixon
Duncan horn $4 in gold from a pail of
ore from his claim there.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MAT 3, 1899.
WILL CONTROL
THE CANNERIES
OF THE STATE
A New Combination of
Fruit-Packers.
TO MAKE A STEADY MARKET
THREE LARGE CONCERNS ARE
STILL HOLDING OUT.
The Corporation Will Endeavor to
Minimiza the Cost of Production
by Effecting Sweeping
i Changes.
Articles of incorporation were filed yes
terday for the California Packers' Asso-
elation, with a capital of two and a half
million dollars.
The new concern is composed of some
of the largest fruit-packing establish
ments In the State and Its object will be
to control and regulate the prices of
canned fruits. It will also ship goods to
all parts of the world. It is organised on
practically the same lines as the Alaska
Packers' Association, and the members
hep-: to be able to minimize the cost of
preparing fruit for shipment and thus
dictate prices.
Under the plan agreed upon it is prob
able that some factories will be closed
altogether and others which now can
fruits of all kinds will limit their output
to a single variety. For instance, facto
ries In cherry-growing districts are now
canning net only cherries but peaches,
pears and apricots. The fruits not raised
In that particular district must be
shipped to the country* often at great ex
pense. After having been canned they
must be reshipped to the point of distri
bution, which may be in the neighborhood
where they were grown. It is the plan of
the corporation i" save this. double ship
ping bill by treating each variety of
fruit in the. portion of the State where it
grows in largest quantities.
The new concern held a meeting yes
terday afternoon and organized with the
election of the following directors: A. D.
Cutler of the Cutting Fruit Packing Com
pany; M. .1. Fontana of Fontana & Co.;
E. 11. Nielson, California Preserving
Company; I". Tillmann Jr., Oakland Pre
serving Company; J. H. Hunt. Hunt Bros.
& Co.; R. R. Bentley, Sacramento Pack
ing Company; I. H. Morse of Kins. Morse
& Co.; W. H. Wright, San Jose Packing
Company; W. J. Hotvhkiss, Russian Riv
er Packing Company; James Madison, A.
F. Tenny Company; J. Streining, Rose
City Canning Company.
The concerns which have not entered
Into the combination are the Southern
California Packing Company, the Pacific
Cannery Company and the California
Canneries Company.
Baker Contest Withdrawn.
The contest to the will of the late Oliver
'H. Baker was withdrawn in Judge
I Troutt's court yesterday and decedent's
will presented for probate by Christian
Caldwell was admitted. A prior will filed
by Thomas E. Haven and Mary A. O'Bri
en was also withdrawn and the estate will
be quietly settled.

PAID A BIG
SUM FOR THREE
DAYS' FREEDOM
The Charge Against
an Attorney.
STRANGLER SUSPECT TALKS
GRAND JURY WILL BE ASKED
TO INVESTIGATE.
Harry Wilson, the Negro, Sentenced
to Eighteen .Months in the
County Jail for Petty
Larceny.
Harry Wilson, the suspected strangler.
who jumped his bonds and was captured
at Santa Cruz, was taken before Judge
Graham yesterday morning to be tried
on the three charges of petty larceny
booked against him. Detectives Ed Gib
son. Wren and McMahon' had In readiness
the three women to testify against him
and others who would have corroborated
their statements, as some of them had
also been victims in the same* way. Wil
son did not put them to the trouble of
testifying, as he pleaded guilty to each
charge, although declaring he was not
guilty on one.
The Judge at once sentenced him to six
months in the County Jail on each charge,
making eighteen months altogether. Af
ter receiving his sentence he asked per
mission to speak to the Judge. He told
the Judge that he was not so bad as he
had been painted and was not cognizant
of the serious crimes which suspicion had
pointed at him. He then said that he had
given Attorney .1. N. E. Wilson $300 to get
him out of th*- City Prison and the attor
ney had told him that the case would be
squared and he need not make his appear
ance in court. He did not know anything
about the bondsmen; all he cared for was
to get his liberty, and he was willing to
pay the $300 for it.
Before court opened yesterday morning
Attorney Wilson made a request at the
chiefs office to see the negro, but was
told that he did not want to see him.
Captain Seymour was persuaded to go
with Attorney Wilson to the prison, and
tin- negro was called out. The negro was
sullen and flatly accused the attorney of
getting the $300 in three $100 hills to get
him out and have the case squared.
Attorney Wilson denied that he got a
cent from the negro, and angrily said:
"Did I not tell you to be in court the next
morning?"
The negro retorted: "No, you did not.
You told me to get out of the city, as the
police expected a Spanish woman from
Panama who would make a serious
charge against me, and I told you that
I did not know anything about a Spanish
woman."
Attorney Wilson again mad.- denial of
getting the money, ami said all he got
was an order on the property clerk for $23
which was in Wilson's pockets when ar
rested.
"Well." said the negro, "it is a negro s
word against a white man's; that's all."
Tie- negro repeated the same story to
Chief Bees and hinted that he had wit
nesses who would prove that he was
right The Chief will lay the matter be
fore the Grand Jury, so that the whole
nauseous business can be probed to the
bottom. __________________
WILL BUILD A NEW
HOME OF VAUDEVILLE
ED FISCHER TO ERECT A HAND-
SOME THEATER.
The Proprietor of the Oberon Is
About to Engage in an Ambi
tious Amusement Venture.
Two hundred thousand dollars Is about
to be Invested in the construction of a
new theater In San Francisco. The pro
moter of the enterprise is Ed Fischer,
proprietor of the Oberon, and the location
chosen for the new playhouse is on O'Far
rell street, adjoining the Alcazar.
For some time contractors have been
at work on the premises. A large three
story building was removed and excava
tion has been made for a basement. The
plans were submitted to contractors yes
terday by the architects, and their esti
mates will i"- finished to-day. Fischer
will push the work with all possible
speed, and by October the doors will be
thrown open to the public.
Vaudeville will till the bills.
For this class of theatricals the plans
will differ somewhat from other specialty
showhouses. There will be three galleries
and a graduated scale of admission prices.
The pit, however, will be built on the or
dinary plan. In general appearance it
will resemble the Tivoli. only its seating
capacity will be much greater. The speci
fications call for a building of 50 feet
frontage and 137:6 feet deep. The cost will
be great, but Fischer says he expects to
realize handsomely on this investment.
Reception to the Twenty-fourth.
A reception will be tendered to the
Twenty-fourth United States Infantry. by
the members and families of the Starr
King A. M. E. Zion Church. 805 Stockton
street, near Clay, this evening at 7:30
o'clock. Addresses will be delivered by
Sergeant Major John R. Green of the
Twenty-fourth Infantry, Sergeant A. J.
Franklin of the Twenty-fourth Infantry.
Company E; Sergeant Henry Bate! of
Company L, Twenty-fourth Infantry:
Corporal Powell of the Twenty-fourth In
fantry, Company E. The celebrated band
of the Twenty-fourth Infantry will be in
attendance and will furnish the music
of the evening. Representative gentlemen
of the race will deliver addresses also.
The public is cordially invited.
Poker Players Dismissed.
Judge Groezinger yesterday discharged
R. O. Thurman. D. Stevens and W. ,F.
Brown, who were arrested on April 30 for
violating section 830 of the Penal Code.
The men admitted playing poker for
money and also to taking out a percent
age, which they asserted was need In buy
ing drinks. In dismissing them, Judge
Groezinger reprimanded the keeper and
the players for participating In the game.

GAS BILLS ARE
RENDERED AT
SAME OLD RATE
Issue Has Not,, as Yet,
Been Forced.
$1 75 IS STILL THE CHARGE
CONSUMERS DO NOT PROFIT BY
RECENT LEGISLATION.
Officials of the Gas Company Affirm
That They Will Never Agree
to the $1 10
Rate.
The hills of the Ran Francisco Gas and
Electric Company for April are due and
will be presented to consumers as rapid
ly as the clerical force of the corporation
can handle them. The rate for which they
will be rendered is Jl 77 per thousand cu
bic feet, the same charge that has been
made for years.
According to Mayor Phelan the com
pany is not entitled to that rate for its
products, as on the 3d of last month the
Supervisors fixed $1 10 as the rate to be
charged the householders. The board re-.
scinded, or endeavored to rescind, this
action afterward, hut his Honor the May
or holds the law does not countenance
such action and that the $1 10 rate must
stand for one year. He takes this posi
tion on a section of the consolidation act,
which provides that rat can be fixed but
once a year. Having acted in the matter
once the board cannot review Its work.
Up to th*- present time no one has ap
peared on the scene ready to force the
issue .-.mi determine the rights of the peo
ple and of the gas company in the prem
ises. The officials of tin company affirm
they have not decided upon the course
they will pursue, but are awaiting the
advice of their attorneys as to the law
bearing on the case, it is thought some
thing may develop at any time that will
take the case Into the courts for them to
pass upon.
Attorney William Rix, who is one of the
legal advisers of the company, in discuss
ing the situation yesterday said: "The
San Francisco Gas and Electric Company
will never agree to accept the rat of
$1 10 per thousand cubic feet. This is
not fair compensation for the service sup
plied. We have two points upon which
to base our fight. First. that the Supervis
ors have no right to fix rates and sec
ond, that if they have the right they have
rescinded their action naming $1 10 as the
amount to be charged. We believe the
latter contention will hold in law. hence
no rate has been fixed by the present
Board of Supervisors and th.- old rate
obtains. The company will not do any
thing wrong, illegal or unjust."
President Crockett said â  the bills for
April an- being made out at the old rate
and that up to th*- present time no one
was objecting very hard. "They know
the company is here and is responsible."
he said, "and whatever rate is finally de
termined upon as th.- legal one the con
sumers will lose nothing. We await our
attorneys' advice in the matter before we
take any steps. They are going thorough
ly int.. the law of th.- case and our direc
tors will be governed by their advice."
.Mr. Crockett Would not discuss the re
duction in price of gas in New Fork on
Monday from $1 10 per thousand cubic
feet to"f*s cents." "1 do not know what the
conditions are In New York." said Mr.
Crockett. "It seems to be a tight between
the different companies and the price
asked for seems to have no relation to
th.- cost of production. It could not prop
erly he hold to he a parallel case to that
in this city."
As the matter stands the rate as fixed
by the Supervisors is $1 l" and this is the
only 'pal charge which th*- company can
make.

ANTIOCH GUN CLUBS
ARE GETTING READY
SEVENTH TOURNAMENT OF THE
TARGET ASSOCIATION.
Men From All Parts of the State Will
Meet in Competition at the An
nual Festival.
Trap shooting is now one of the lead
ing outdoor sporting features which has
numerous supporters in all parts of the
State. â  -b
Four years ago a few enthusiastic
sportsmen in different parts of th.- coun
try, desiring to develop the trap shoot
ing skill of individuals and to promote
acquaintance and good fellowship among
sportsmen, met and organized the Cali
fornia Inanimate Target Association. Un
der its auspices six successful trap shoot
ing tournaments have been held at San
Francisco, Stockton, Alameda. San Jose
and Monterey. Now the seventh tourna
ment, which promises to eclipse in every
particular all previous tournaments, will
be held at Antloch on the 29th and 30th of
this month.
The programme of individual events
will provide for five twenty-bird races
each day. Class shooting is to prevail
and a trophy is to be provided as the
first-class prize in every event and the
prize money will be divided between the
successful competitors in the four classes.
Heretofore it has been the custom where
four moneys were given to allow 40 per
cent to the first class, 'â 'â <) per cent to sec
ond class, 20 per cent to third class and
.10 per cent to fourth class. But this year
the old way of doing businessâ that is. in
segregating tin- money In prizesâ ls to be
reversed, so that 10 per cent will go to
the first class, 20 to the second class. 30
to the third class and 40 to the fourth
class. Th.- philosophy of this division is
t.i place the greatest amount of money
where there will be th.- greatest number
of men to share its division.
The trophies to be shot for this year are
very valuable and handsome. The Roos
"three-man team" trophy will be con
tested for at Antloch for the third time,
and this promises to be one of the hotest
races of the tournament. A new trophy
will ,be provided for the "six-man teams'*
and th*- usual cash prises will be given
for high averages. Decorations will also
be provided for the best two averages be
tween SO and 90 per cent, three for the
best three averages between 70 and 80 per
cent and four for the best four averages

McGLADE IS ON TRIAL.
His Counsel Making a Technical
Fight for His Liberty.
The trial of Peter McGlade, ex-hook
keeper of the Street Superintendent's of
fice, charged with forging demands on the
treasury, opened before Judge Cook yes
terday morning. McGlade was put on trial
for the specific crime charged to him of
forging a warrant for $96. purporting to
be the demand of one H. Swords for labor
performed on behalf of the city. Ex-Su
perintendent of Streets Ambrose was put
upon the stand and testified that McGlade
was employed in his oilice at the time the
alleged crimes were committed Swords
then took the stand and said that at the
time of the forgery of the warrant he
was not employed by the city, consequent
ly could make no demand on the city. At
this point the prosecution endeavored to
introduce the forged . document in evi
dence, and here the technical fight began.
Patrick Red. ly objected to the introduc
tion of the document on various grounds,
and in order to argue the points raised
the Jury was dismissed until this morn
ing, at which time Judge Cook will rule
on the merits of the points raised.

THOSE SEIZED CIGARS.
Holders Will Be Let Off as Lightly
as May Be Consistent With
the Good of the Service.
The Commissioner of Internal Revenue
has notified Internal Revenue Collector
Lynch that it being apparent that the
holders of cigars stamped with the coun
terfeit revenue stamps, with which the
country was flooded by an enterprising
firm in Pennsylvania, were innocent of
wrong intent, they will he dealt with ns
lightly as may ho consistent with the
good of the service, the t.olleetor havinc:
requested that such a course be pursued.
The cigars will ho returned to the persons
from whom they were taken, on the pay
ment by them of the sum necessary to
replace th*- counterfeit stamps with good
ones, and of a nominal fine, presumably
for. the purpose of sharpening their eye
sight.
Bight thousand cigars with counterfeit
stamps were seized last Monday in
Fresno, and the Deputy Collectors all
over the State are making a strict search
for more.
was to be placed on the fastest
sprinter that over put on the spikes.
Tho wise man had programmed the
affair to a nicety. He had persuaded
a number of Stockton men that they
might have money to sell If they
would make a match with their favor
ite sprinter, whose nnm de guerre is
Gibson. All that was required of
them was their presence in Pleasan
ton on the day fixed with pouches
and wallets.
The sprinter who was to win the
boodle for the local sports was
brought to this city, and he did mar
velous work at the Olympic Club
track. The local contingent couldn't
lose. They left on Tuesday night for
the scene of pedal strife. On the train
the sprinter and the rest made things
lively for a while, until he of the
spikes found it necessary to go into
another car, as the smoke was crawl
ing down his lungs and he feared for
his wind. This was a good sign of
earnestness, and the San Francisco
contingent admired the honesty of
their principal.
At Pleasanton the Stockton crowd
met the local force and gave them
assistance in deporting the empty
pouches which the latter had brought
to pack off the money of the former.
The race was run on Friday morning,
and it was as pretty a race as any
sportsman could hope for. The bet
ting was brisk, the local band having
brought about $2000 to the track. Mc-
Henery held the stakes and Fred
Vetter was a judge. The San Fran
cisco idol took the lend In the first
ten yards: then the Stockton wonder
forged ahead and set the pace. About
the sixth second. San Francisco was
in the lead again and country money
was on its way to the metropolis.
Just then something happened which
Delaney docs not care to describe.
Stockton won by an inch.
The sprinters did not stop running
when they finished their ; 100-yard
course. The San Francisco man kept
moving right along until he reached
a buggy, which moved him further
along and out of the way of a great
wrath. The Stockton people became
suddenly jubilant and the San Fran
cisco contingent began looking for
rifles. But their spike wonder was a
hummer, and he ran the race of his
life, It would have been his last had
they caught him, which they did not.
Let no man ask John Delaney if he
has pouches for sale.
BULLMAN GETS
ANOTHER FALL
ON THE TRACK
This Time While Rid
ing Cromwell.
i _
HORSE BLED AND COLLAPSED
! I DON'T KNOW FINALLY
EARNED BRACKETS.
I
I -
| Cheap Card and Light Attendance at
Oakland â Three Favorites Fin
ished First â Out
sprinted His Field.
i
| Bullman. the popular local rider, re-
I ceived another severe fall at Oakland
! track yesterday. While piloting Cromwell
! In the seven-furlong event, fifth on the
I card, the chestnut horse became afflicted
i with his old habit of bleeding and fell on
j the back stretch, near the flve furlong
j pole. The Cambridge stable representa
tive ruled second choice in the hotting
1 and Bullman had set out to make the
running. Suddenly his mount was Been
to falter and fall back, then fall. Bull
man was at first supposed to be badly In
jured, but an examination developed the
fact that he had escaped with some
bruises and a shaking up.
The programme was not an enticing one,
attracting a small crowd. Three favorites
were bowled over.
A big field of seventeen platers started
in the first event, a five furlong scramble.
Bill Dingley, the Montana rider, had the
mount on i Don't Know, against which
sprinter 12 to 1 was laid, and In a fierce
drive with Madrone earned the verdict
by a nose. The favorite. Polka, showed
no speed whatever.
The chestnut colt Roy Hooker ruled a
pronounced favorite in the betting
on the six furlong spin that followed, but
was outfooted from the start. Holme;,
kept Limewater in the lead until the wire
was neared, when he was passed and led
out by Bullman, astride Jennie Reid,
against which 4 to 1 was laid.
A band of cheap ones lined up in the
mile selling run. Fnrtis. the mount of
Bullman, had a slight call over Soraia In
the betting, and coming on from fifth po
sition in the stretch won cleverly from
the 200 to 1 shot, Flacon.
Indifferently handled by Jenkins, Plan,
the 6 to 7. choice for the mile and a six
teenth run, never looked dangerous. The
Fretter made all the running and won
cleverly from Whaleback. a 10 to 1 out
sider. Tempo finished third.
Rosinante took the seven furlong affair
after a hard drive with Tony I.icalzi.
Coffey's mare was a 3 to 5 favorite, and.

Track Notes.
This will be the closing day of the Cali
fornia Jockey Club's season of racing. A
Darktown derby will be the feature of the
card.
Hahn, a well-developed specimen of the
"genus pinhead," which flourish in pro
fusion on racetracks, was fined $25 by the
judges for boating The Plunger over the
head with his whip.
Presiding Judge Murphy departed for
St. Louis last evening, where he will have
the same position at the Fair Grounds.

Rroke Her Leg.
Miss J. McLean, a servant, residing at
3409 Webster street, broke her leg last
evening while alighting from a Mission
street car at the corner of Ninth street.
She was removed to the Receiving Hos
pital and attended to by Dr. Thompson.
The injured woman said the accident was
due to a misstep.
