THE BUG IN THE CHARTER.
\A R. HENRY X. CLEMENT and Dr. E. R.
/ \ Taylor are able and reputable lawyers. In
* * The Call of Sunday these gentlemen as
signed two reasons for the failure to provide in the
new charter for the regulation of the rates and
charges of the Pacific Telephone and Telegraph Com
pany, and for the discrimination between that cor
poration and other monopolies, classified as common
carriers. One of these reasons is the doubtful con
stitutionality of such municipal legislation, and the
other the supposed intermixture of the business of
the Pacific Telephone and Telegraph Company, which
is a local concern, with the service of the Sunset Tele
phone and Telegraph Company, extending to all parts
of the State:
It is not the function of the press to determine c0n
..... T , , , , ,
stttutional questions. It had been supposed, however,
â â .â â .â â â .-â â â â ..-â â â  â  â 
that there could be no doubt of the power of State
regulation m the particulars mentioned, and that the
only point in controversy was the existence of this
right in the municipality, either by express specifica
tion or under the implications of the law. We have
not before us any direct adjudication on the subject,
but in the American Law Review for April-May, 1896,
a very able opinion by one of the most prominent
lawyers in Kansas was published, in which the rights
of State control and of the delegation of municipal
control over telephone companies was vigorously as
serted. In Massachusetts also, perhaps in other
*v* : 'yy . â  _.
States, the same views are entertained. It seems that
all monopolies or even competitive associations, de
pending upon franchises or privileges derived from
the public, should be subject to restrictions in their
exactions and should not be permitted equally to
plunder the community at large and their private cus
tomers.
This municipality has its regular legal adviser, to
whom the constitutional question should be promptly
committed. If the State constitution as it exists
manacles the Legislature in dealing with telephone
companies it should be amended. We do not believe
that there is any such limitation. The other point
suggested by Mr. Clement and Dr. Taylor can hardly
be sustained. There can be no doubt that the State
â¢
jurisdiction of the Sunset Telephone and Telegraph
Company and the municipal jurisdiction of the Pacific
Telephone and Telegraph Company are clearly dis
tinguishable. To a certain extent they may use the
same wires and the same instruments. But it is well
understood that the identity of each corporation is
separately maintained, that each service is independent
of the other, that the accounts are not commingled,
and that it is entirely practicable to treat the business
of the Pacific Telephone and Telegraph Company as
distinct and complete within itself.
The existence of this bug in the new charter, to
which The Call has invited the attention of the pub
lic and of the municipal authorities, is unquestion
able. The constitutional doubt should have been re
solved in favor of the people and not of the telephone
monopoly. There should be no delay or hesitation
in the exercise of lawful means to compel its submis
sion to reasonable supervision over its tariff and to
some recognition of the interests of its customers.
These monopolies, in alliance with the railroad, have
virtually held themselves above the law in this State
and have treated the public with contempt. It is time
to make them understand that the American people
live under a free Government and not under a private
despotism.
In their efforts to distribute prize money to the
Cubans the American authorities are having a great
deal of trouble with "swollen" lists of insurgent
Cub m soldiers. When the American Government
needed Cuban soldiers in the war with Spain the list
of available and willing men was lamentably small.
From the tenor of British comment on Coghlan's
speech it seems the opinion over there is that he
ought to be deprived of his rank as captain, retired
for about a couple of weeks and then restored to the
navy as a commodore.
Senator Butler of North Carolina is said to have
enrolled himself as a student in the law school of the
university of the State, which shows that the Senatot,
though a Populist, has sense enough to see that he
can never learn any law in the Senate.
The emissaries of Aguinaldo want to retire from
their rebellion with honor. Thus far they have retired
flora the advance of the American troops with haste,
It is rather late in the campaign for them to change
their policy.
It is cheering to hear that the Emperor William
has joined in the gayety of nations. He has just read
and apparently enjoyed "Hoch der Kaiser 1"
The report that Aguinaldo is of Irish parentage on
his father's side accounts for his excellence as a
.fighter, but where did he get that name?
APPOINTED SENATORS.
SENATOR PERKINS, in discussing the seating
of Senators who are appointed by the execu
tive,' says he may be criticized for his present
I position because he voted to seat Corbett of Oregon.
' The first case decided by the Senate was that
of Kensey Johns, appointed by the Governor
of Delaware in 1794, after a legislative ses
sion had been held subsequent to the occur
rence of the vacancy. The Senate held that the
Legislature having met, the Governor took no juris
diction by its failure to elect a Senator. Up to this
time this rule has been adhered to. The issue raised
in the Corbett case, however, was new. A careful
study of the discussion proves this plainly. The
case was stated by Senator Spooner of Wisconsin, an
able lawyer. It turned on what constitutes "a meet
ing" of the Legislature. The Legislature of Oregon
met and the Senate organized permanently for the
transaction of business. The House never reached
even a temporary organization, but met from day to
day without a quorum, in a futile effort to get a
quorum and permanently organize. In this way the
time allotted to the session passed away without the
transaction of any legislative business, and, of course,
without notice to the Governor that the Legislature
was in session at all, since the official notification to
him of that fact by committee never occurred. The
issue in the Corbett case, then, was. Does such a
gathering of the members elected to the Legislature
constitute a meeting of that body within the legal and
constitutional meaning?
Senator Spooner said: "The constitution has de
volved that Federal function (election of a Senator)
upon the Legislature of a State. So it is but a step
to argue, and the conclusion is inevitable, that
there is no jurisdiction under the constitution
of the United States to perform this Federal
function until, by compliance with the consti
tution of the State and the law of the State,
there exists an organized legislative body, consti
tuting a Legislature capable of legislating. So it was,
at the suggestion of Mr. Fessenden, in 1866, that the
present law was passed as we find it in the statutes to
day, containing the words, 'meeting and organization
of the Legislature.' Now, what is the 'organization'
of a Legislature? I looked at Anderson's Law Dic
tionary this morning and it says: 'In the sense of to
constitute, to qualify for the exercise of appropriate
functions. It may refer to a government, a court, a
legislative body, a board of deputies or other officers.'
"If there is a quorum of sworn members in each
House, who have elected a Speaker or a President
pro tempore, who have provided themselves with fa
cilities for expressing and recording the legislative
will, who have clothed themselves by a performance
of conditions precedent with the power to legislate, so
that they may pass laws which will be valid and bind
ing, a rule of action upon the people of the State,
even though they may not have been able to elect a
permanent Speaker they should be taken as a Legisla
ture within the constitutional sense and qualified to
choose a Senator." ,
The Oregon House consists of sixty members. The
constitution of the State requires that two-thirds of
each House shall constitute a quorum to do busi-
ness, but a smaller number may meet and compel at
tendance of absentees and adjourn from day to day.
Observe the language, "may" â not "must" nor
"shall" â compel attendance of absentees. At the first
meeting of the House thirty-four were present and
twenty-six absent. When the roll was called thirty
responded and thirty were absent. So it continued
from day to day, the highest number responding to
rollcall being thirty-one. There was not even a tem
porary organization, no temporary Speaker was
elected, and only thirty-one of the sixty members were
even sworn in.
Senator Spooner's argument that this did not con
stitute ''a meeting and organization of the Legisla
ture" was perfectly correct, and his deduction there
from that the Legislature not having met and organ
ized the Governor had not lost jurisdiction of the
Senatorial appointment was highly plausible and
justifiable. But, observe that very argument was a
complete admission that if the Legislature had met
and had organized and had then failed to elect a Sen
ator the Governor's jurisdiction would have been lost.
Therefore, in voting to seat Corbett Senator Perkins
remained in line with the adjudicated cases of Johns
and Lanman, and his vote was not inconsistent with
his present position. The doctrine of the constitution
is that the Governor takes jurisdiction only when the
vacancy occurs during a recess of the Legislature,
that such jurisdiction ceases upon the meeting and or
ganization of the Legislature and does not return to
the Governor upon legislative failure to exercise it.
It was plausibly held in the Corbett case, in line
with the foregoing doctrine, that the Governor's juris
diction remains intact if the Legislature fail to "or
ganize."
As we have said before, the whole matter is so
plain that it needs no lawyer to state it. The Legis
latures of California, Utah, Delaware and Pennsyl
vania had met, organized and were in session transact
ing legislative business, and the executives were offi
cially notified and cognizant thereof when the vacan
cies occurred in the Senate by expiration of the
regular terms. Therefore jurisdiction was never in
the Governors of those States, but was originally in
the Legislatures and remains there until 'the Legis
latures choose to exercise it.
Th|E PEACE CONGRESS.
WHILE we have welcomed May this year not
with a festival of rejoicing over the return of
the season of flowers, but with the celebration
of a great epoch-making victory, the month will not
be lacking in- more peaceful suggestions, for its chief
event will be the gathering of the international con
ference called by the Czar to consider the possibility
of bringing about a general disarmament of the
nations.
The conference is to open at The Hague on the
18th and every civilized nation on the globe will be
represented among its members. The delegates from
this country will be Andrew D. White, Embassador
to Germany; Stanford Newel, Minister to the Neth
erlands; Professor Seth Low of Columbia Univer
sity; Captain Crozier of the United States army, and
Captain Mahan of the navy. The American commis
sion as a whole is regarded as an exceptionally strong
body, several of its members being men of interna
tional reputation.
It is not expected that much of a positive nature
will be accomplished by the conference, for no dele
gation is to be empowered to do or to promise any
thing which will be binding upon its Government. It
is to be a meeting for discussion merely, and from the
tone of the comment on the subject in diplomatic
circles it is doubtful if the Czar's suggestion of gen
eral^ disarmament will be given much more than a
perfunctory consideration.
Nevertheless there is a strong belief the discussions
at the meeting will result in materially strengthening
the cause of peace. It is said the United States and
Great Britain will unite in urging an international
agreement that private property shall in times of war
be respected on sea as on land, and will also advo
TILE SAJS FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MAY 3, 1899.
cate a more general adoption of arbitration as a
means of settling international disputes.
The notable feature of the conference will be the
part the United States will play in it. For the first
time an American delegation will sit in a great Euro
pean council and be respected as the representative of
a world power of the first class. Of course American
sentiment with respect to disarmament will have little
weight, since that issue does not affect us. We have
no heavy armaments to menace Europe, neither do
the armaments of European nations menace us; but
on all other questions the expression of the Ameri
can Commissioners will be given as much attention
as is paid to those of any other power.
It is generally believed in â  Europe that after the
conference at The Hague is over there will be a meet
ing of the greater rulers of Europe in England, at
which time the recommendations of the conference
will be reviewed and possibly some agreement may
be reached which will strengthen that famous "con
cert of Europe," which has now become an established
institution of diplomacy. Under any circumstances
the peace conference is likely to be the most impor
tant event of the year, and its proceedings will be
studied closely by all who are interested in the wel
fare of nations and the prosperity of mankind.
UNDER our free institutions the people make the
laws, subject only to essential constitutional re
strictions. The enforcement of the law, there
fore, is guaranteed by a compact between the people
themselves, which cannot be disregarded without
vital danger to the republic.
The Call has uniformly insisted that the Federal
constitution and the State constitutions/ and all the
laws made in pursuance thereof should be rigidly
observed. It has applied this obviously correct doc
trine without evasion or partiality. It has demanded
its application to its own party and to the Federal
administration. It has denounced Asiatic expansion
and imperialism, because the one is a substantial
equivalent for the other and both are repugnant to
constitutional obligations and also to the theory and
the practice of our Government. While recognizing
the terrible provocations in the South, it has uni-.
formly resisted the cruelties of mobs and declared
that crime should be punished through the courts,
whether perpetrated by brutalized negroes or by re
vengeful whites. It has antagonized monopoly in
every form that has raised it above the law or enabled
it to strike at the rights and the interests of the pub
lic, whether represented by oppressive trusts or wield
ing dictatorial power through an individual, as in the
case of C. P. Huntington.
The scenes in Idaho, which have been depicted
vividly in our columns, show the evil example and the
viciousness of unrepressed lawlessness. In the most
wanton manner, without excuse or palliation, valuable
and wealth-producing property has been destroyed
and hundreds â perhaps thousands of industrious
citizens at least temporarily deprived of employment.
The crime is as deadly to labor as it is to capital.
Equality before the law does not mean an aristocracy
of violence any more than it means an aristocracy of
fraud or greed. It does mean the same law and the
same administration of the law for the rich and the
poor alike, and that all men of every condition, living
in the United States and possessed of the inestimable
and inalienable rights of American citizenship, or
whose presence among us is tolerated, shall be com
pelled to live in peace and in order, in conformity
with the necessities of civilized society, and shall not
attempt to steal their neighbor's goods, to injure his
body, to take his life, nor forcibly to redress their own
grievances.
Mob law spreads like an epidemic. It must be
dealt with dispassionately and temperately, but with
inexorable firmness and promptness. If General Otis
is justified in refusing to treat with the Filipinos un
til they have unconditionally surrendered, there is
much greater necessity for the refusal to temporize
with outlaws in an established American State upon
this continent. Lawless combinations of miners at
Wardner are quite as destructive and inexcusable as
aggressive and fraudulent combinations of capital in
San Francisco or in New York.
UNIVERSITY REORGANIZATION.
T F the report so widely circulated be true that- East-
IF the authorities consulted upon the that- East
ern authorities consulted upon the selection
of a new president for the State University have
advised that upon the close of the first academic year
after the new man enters upon his work all the pro
fessors and heads of departments in the university
shall resign so as to leave him free to reorganize the
institution, then the Regents are certainly wise in
deliberating well and long before they act. A whole
sale reorganization and reconstitution of the univer
sity staff may be attractive, but it will be a hazardous
experiment to carry out.
The claim is made by those who favor the rumored
plan that there are in the university some professors
who should never have been appointed and some who
have grown old in the service and are no longer use
ful. The elimination of these, it is said, would be of
great benefit to the institution. New men would
mean new vigor, new energy and a new. and more
vitalizing stimulus to the university in all lines of its
work.
Even if all that is claimed were sure to be accom
plished beyond a shadow of a doubt, there would still
be serious objections to anything like a wholesale
sweeping of the university by an Eastern broom after
but one year of experience in the State. It would be
demoralizing to university life if each change in the
presidency should mean a wholesale change in the
professorships and the teaching body generally.
Under such conditions a university career would be
almost as uncertain as one in politics, and a professor
in future would have to give a good deal of time and
energy to the work of wire-pulling among the powers
that be in order to hold his position after attaining it.
The great universities of Europe and the East have
not become centers of learning and noted as the
homes of scholarship by any practice of turning pro
fessors out of their chairs simply because they have
grown old in service, or because they arc not "up
to date" according to the ideas of some new head of
the institution.
There is no need for a clean sweep at Berkeley, and
if there is to be one it should not be intrusted to an
Easterner. Berkeley should, have a strong and vig
orous personality in the president's chair, but there is
no reason why she should seek it at the risk of de
moralizing the staff or rendering uncertain the tenure
of cffice of the men who elect to serve her in any of
her departments.
It begins to look as if the Filipinos were awaken
ing to a realization of the fact that American bullets
are hard to dodge and don't bounce after they strike.
Up to date about a dozen candidates have come
forward to fill Tom Reed's place as Speaker of the
House, and still there is room for more.
The dispatches announce that the Coghlan incident
has been duly closed. It is possible that some covert
reference is made to the gallant captain's mouth.
ENFORCE THE LAW
NEWS OF THE MINES.
One of the few Argonauts now living
who were present at the birth of quartz
mining on Gold Mountain, near Grass Val
ley, late in 1850, is Frank Morse, who is
an engineer at the San Francisco Mint.
His interest in the nebulous history of
that beginning of quartz mining was
greatly stimulated by an account re
cently published in these columns in con
nection with the Peabody-Gold Hill suit,
involving the oldest quartz mine in the
State. The following is part of the story
told by Mr. Morse:
"I suppose there are not more . than
three or four, if as many, survivors of
that meeting of December 30, 1850, when
the Gold Mountain mining district was or
ganized. I remember the time quite well.
I went out there in .September, 1850, from
Parks Bar, on the Yuba near Marysville,
where I had been running a wheelbarrow
for $16 a day until I got a fever, as so
many of the miners did.
"This rich ledge on Gold Mountain was
known to exist for some months befote
this organization of the district, and the
recording of claims. The ledge was
broken off and turned downhill like a sort
of wave and it was not found in place
short of about forty feet below the sur
face. The side of the hill below the vein
and the ravine were rich and claims were
being worked when I got there, each
claim being 30 by 40 feet. The rules ob
served elsewhere in nearby diggings were
followed, but claims were not recorded
until December 30, 1850, when laws Were
adopted and a recorder elected. Thomas
Cracklin's claim merely happened to be
the flrst one recorded and so ranks as the
oldest one by the record. Some of the
rock of the broken vein was very rich
and, of course, the dirt was rich. The
only way most of us could work the rock
at first was by pounding up the richest
specimens on a stone with a hammer and
then panning out. A man by the name of
Huff, who 1 thnik was an old Georgia
miner, had a big mortar and it was the
only one in the diggings. He and his
party rigged up a spring pole and pOund
ed out $20,000 that winter. Long years af
ter I found Huff ranching on the Ameri
can River. To show haw much we knew
about quartz mining, a few of us pooled
our claims and run a tunnel 250 feet long
that tapped the ledge about- forty feet
below the surface. There was no water
to contend with and we could much better
have just sunk a shaft forty feet deep,
but we had an idea that we ought to have
a tunnel for some reason, probably be
cause it would be easier to get the rock
out. 1 remember that Joseph Cracklin
took out $15,000 with a mortar and went
East in 1851. I helped him pound it out.
Two men, Walsh and Wheeler, bought up
a number of the claims that winter ana
turned their sawmill into a quartz mill.
They afterward sold to a London com
pany, who sent out Melville Attwood as
manager, about 1852. -?. â¢ ....
"'Some of those lirst mills built in 1861
would be great curiosities now. One was
set up near my cabin, and would work
night and dayâ if they could get It to
work. I would be trying to sleep in
my cabin and would hear them say, 'Well,
boys, I guess we've got steam enough
now.' Then the thing would start up and
go set-choo, rattlety-bang, rattlety
bang, rattle-ty-bang, rat-tle-ty-bang, sst,
choo â bang*, and then it would stop. The
Kelly mill was not very far away,
and was made almost entirely of wood.
The wooden mortars were lined with
boiler iron. As I remember the cam shaft
it was a big lug 18 inches thick with the
wooden cams mortised in a spiral row
around it. As the journals were all of
wood, it would squeak frightfully. You
could hear it a mile sometimes. When it
would squeak a fellow would climb up
and 'dope' the bearings with molasses.
1 suppose they did not have any grease
to spare. A lot of the rock ore we
took out of our tunnel and sent to the
first mill went only $49 a ton, and as we
had to pay $50 a ton to have it crushed,
we quit."
Herbert Long, an emintent mining au
thority, has written for the New York
Engineering and Mining Journal an ex
tensive and interesting account of the
Iron Mountain mine of the Mountain Cop
per Company, in Shasta County, which is
being operated on bo large a scale.
The ore deposit, he Bays, is part of a
great lode, in some places hundreds . of
feet wide. The mine proper consists of a
great lens of ore roughly shaped; like a
ship's hull that has been . thoroughly
mapped and explored, It is estimated to
contain 1,750,000 tons of sulphides. There
is an excellent prospect of finding similar
ore bodies to the north of it. The fol
lowing is an interesting estimate of the
value of the mine:
"Assuming the correctness of Mr. Hill's
estimates of quantity and richness, the
sulphides contain very nearly 129,000 short
tons of metallic copper. The sulphides
and oxides together have over $3. 0ut), 000 in
gold, and S.tKHhiHH. ounces silver, assuming
there are 1.000.000 tons of workable limo
nlte. Th* total value at present market
prices of the three valuable metals would
be something over $53,000,000. This is
quite enough to make a good mine.
â  "After prolonged study of th*' mine and
its ores 1 concluded that it would require
ten years for the exhaustion of the known
ore body, and that by working on the
plans which I submitted to the company,
an annual net profit of $1,300,000 would be
secured, or $1.1.000.000 in all. The present
worth of this Mini might very reasonably
be taken as the value of the mine, and
this, at the ruling rates of interest, would
be about $9,000,000. This estimate, deal
ing only with the known resources, is
moderate when â â >. recognize the prob
abilities of the remainder of the great
area which is not yet fully prospected,
but in which ore is known to exist in no
small quantities.
"It can hardly be believed that this mine
laid unappreciated, though not unknown,
through so many years, while an active
search for mineral went on through this
neighborhood and all over the State. For
years, the Iron Mountain mine was
hawked about for a buyer unsuccessfully,
end was eventually sold for $300,000, part
stock, pari cash, to the present owners,
the Mathesons of London, and their asso
ciates."
The news comes this week that the Col
orado company, which is just starting one
great gold dredger on a bench of the
American River at Mississippi Bar six
miles from Folsom. 150 feet above the
river, is planning to construct tin-. or
four more in that region. Other dredging
enterprises are in process of elaboration,
and the Increase of dredgers promises '<<
be rapid. This form of the industry his
great possibilities in California, but like
anything in mining must be handled with
skill and discretion.
Mr. Postlewftite, who is California's
leading authority on the subject, says
"The field for dredging in California is
an immense one. Nearly all the rivers
draining into the Sacramento will pay to
dredge in. parts. There is virgin ground
in the beds. Then the old deposits of tail
ings are often rich, for they lost a good
deal of the poM then. Besides the
benches and old cuannels there are many
basins up in the mountains that would
pay well. Where there is a natural or ar
tificial supply of water within twelve feet
of the surface ground can he worked.
"Ground must first be thoroughly pros
pected. To sink shafts with timbering: and
pumping is costly, if not Impossible. There
is now in use a steam traction prospecting
machine, costing $2000. which will sink
and case a hole several inches in diameter
at a cost of about $3 a foot. Ten to twenty
holes should be sunk in an area of 100
acres, according to the amount of
variations. By sinking, such holes
the average values can be closely
known: there are no risks, and it be
comes a legitimate and safe enterprise,
with no element of speculation.
"Under ordinarily favorable conditions
the cost of dredging will be within three
cents a yard: Hard ground costs more,
because less is handled. Bowlders are
handled, but they lessen the output, and if
the.re are too many large ones. I would
say, 'Don't dredge.' One beauty of a
large amount of ground near Oroville is
that the bed rock is a volcanic ash,
which is easily dug up. It will all be 1
dredged some time. It is noticeable that
most of the money being invested is
Eastern money. thouph Californians are
beginning to make- many Inquiries."

In reply to a Los Angeles (Cal.) con
cern that wants to buy from some one
In California 1000 tons of graphite equal
to Ceylon quality, the Mining and Scien
tific Press says that it is believed the or
der cannot lie filled. Practically graphite
does not exist in California in commercial
quantities. There were several San Fran
cisco dealers, but none have produced any
of consequence for a 'ong time. The Cal
ifornia Graphite Company has a deposit
near Cazadero, Sonoma County, but it
has not produced any. The Elkhardt mine
is near Watsons, Mendocino County,
twenty-eight miles from Ukiah, but is a
non-producer, being of too low grade to
be used. There are several small known
deposits in Southern California, none of
which have been utilized.
A mining district that is attracting.con
siderable attention just now lies over in
Nevada to the east of Modoc County, oc
cording to the Redding Free Press. It is
locally known as the Pueblo district, and
is directly on the route of the old over
land immigrant trail. A number of rich
silver and copper strikes have been made
in the district and the sale of one mine,
the Denio, a copper proposition, has lately
been effected. The sale price was the
round sum of $100,000. II is stated that in
early days immigrant trains brought into
California and Oregon samples of ore
extremely rich in silverâ so rich, in fact,
that the white metal would run out in
big drops under the blowpipe. It is a for
bidding district to .he prospector, how
ever, owing to the scarcity of water and
lack of transportation facilities.

agencies of Trinity County. A copper

ty. Several deposits in that county are

TRUMAN CONTEMPT CASE.
Supreme Justices Suggest That Man
damus Would Have Been
Better. .
The case of I. ,T. Truman, the Treasurer,
on habeas corpus was on hearing before
the Justices of the Supreme Court yes
terday and taken under advisement. Con
siderable interest has been manifested in
the matter, because it is not every day
that the Treasurer of a city is adjudged
guilty of contempt of court. Joseph P.
Stevens Is the official reporter in the de
partment of the Superior Court presided
over by Judge Cook. The latter gave, the
reporter an order on the treasury for $117
salary due. but the Treasurer refused to
honor the same. Ha claimed that there
was no money In the fund from which
such orders or warrants are cashed.
Judge Cook asserted that there was am
ple money, for Instance, the general fund,
in the possession of the Treasurer for the
payment of court reporters and he found
Treasurer Truman guilty of contempt for
refusing to honor the demand a".. 1 ordered
tin- city official into tin. custody of the
Sheriff until the money was paid. To this
decision Mr. Truman took exceptions and
appealed for relief to the Supreme Court.
lie obtained a writ of habeas corpus and
when the matter was up for consideration
yesterday li.' took th.- position that he
COUld not legally pay the amount of the
order from the funds set aside for that
purpose. Also that tha order should have
been passe. upon by the Auditor before
the Treasurer could honor it. While the
higher court reserved its decision it in
timated that perhaps mandamus proceed
ings would have served the purpose of the
lower court better Than contempt pro
ceedings. 7_ ; -: . .7.V 7-;;
Reports Received on Indian Work.
The San Francisco Protestant Indian
Association held Its quarterly meeting at
717 Post street yesterday afternoon. Mrs.
J. D. Spencer occupied the chair and Miss
M C. Willey acted as secretary. Reports
were read from Pliny E. Goddard. super
intendent of the Indian mission at Fall
River Mills, Humboldt County, and
showed much advancement in the work
of civilizing and educating the dark
skins. A motion was made that a Christ
mas box be prepared and forwarded to
the reservation. Material contributed for
the preparation thereof may be left at 132
McAllister street. Any material for fancy
or lace work may be contributed at the
same address. . yf-'f-f: y

Rev. '""â¢ Cooke, the recently deposed
pastor of Trinity Church, is preparing for
an early departure, and will leave shortly
lor his former home In New Hampshire.
blnce the reverend gentleman and the
â vestry agreed to disagree there has been
much gossiping as to the whys and
â¢wherefores that caused the vestry to act
po summarily. It [ a known thai dissatis
faction with the pastor dated back many
months and that Dr. Cooke was not
ignorant of the dissension his presence in
the church caused among the parishion
ers. At that early flay Dr. Cooke earn
estly desired an Investigation and offered
to withdraw if thai appeared to the
vestry to be the wisest plan. In the tem
pest in the Trinity teapot Dr. Cooke found
some warm adherents, but many dissatis
fied ones who resented the Innovations he
was pleased to make and In general found
fault with the pastor, his methods and
his personal tastes. It is alleged that
even his love for the dainty and the
beautiful fell under their displeasure, and
they grumblingly complained when the
man who should have been their spiritual
advisor tied a fragrant hunch of blos
soms to the handle-bar of his bicycle.
Dr. Cooke is the son of Jaj Cooke, the
noted financier, and consequently well
provided with this world's Roods; yet
when the reverend gentleman expressed
himself as not In the church for salary
those who made that salary possible
looked upon this statement as a personal
affront and registered another 'kick"
against their pastor.
However, the vestry has chosen to over
look Dr. Cooke's monetary principles and
have paid over to the gentleman $750â $2.">0
salary for the month, allowed as a vaca
tion, and $.100 as a godspeed.
No action has as yet been taken to ap
point a successor to Dr. Cooke. It is a
matter In which the vestry has decided
to act with extreme care. Rev. Mr. Han
son will till the pulpit until a man is
chosen, and it has been decided that there
is no cause for hurry in the matter.
A debt of $2000 hung over the church
when Dr. Cooke was call( and during
his administration the debt has grown
to J7OOO. These figures, however, are not
causing the vestry any anxiety, and they
claim there will be no trouble in canceling
the entire debt. One cause for genuine re
joicing is the return of C. V. S. Gibbs to
the church. Mr. Gibbs was one of Dr.
Cooke's most bitter enemiesâ an enmity
not founded upon any personal dislike but
based entirely upon Mr. Glbbs' inability
to hear and understand the pastor.
Gibbs, who. by the way. is the only one
left of the original founders of he church.
did not give up his pew during Cooke's
term, but discontinued to attend worship
at the church he waa instrumental in
founding nearly fifty .years ago.
PRETTY HOUSE WEDDING.
Harry M. Campe and Miss Annie J.
Hoffman Are United in
Marriage.
Harry M. Campe and Miss Annie J.
Hoffman were married yesterday after
noon by Rev. J. H. Sehroeder it the resi
dence of the parents of the bridegroom,
20 Devisadero street. The ceremony took
place in the parlor, which was handsomely
dt-corattd for the occasion, only the imme
diate friends and relatives of the contract
ing parties being present. The bride was
attended by h*-r sister, Miss Sadie Hoff
man, as bridesmaid, and J. A. Klein was
best man. The couple were preceded to
the extemporized altar by Master George
Campe and Irma tegelken, bearing beau
tiful bouquets of flowers. Immediately
after the ceremony an informal reception
was held, followed by a wedding break
fast, at the conclusion of which the happy
couple departed on a wedding tour
through Lake and Mendocino counties.
They will return to this city In about
three weeks and will be at home at 1128
Hayes street.
The bride and groom are both natives
of San Francisco. The former is engaged
in business with his father, H. Campe,
the wholesale wine and liquor dealer at
2:H-22fi Front street. He Is president of
the Travelers' Protective Association of
the United States, past president of Al
catraz Parlor. N*. S. G. W. - ! member of the
Elks and member of Occidental Lodge, F.
and A. M. Mrs. Campe is the daughter
of Mrs. Catherine Hoffman, 1519 Howard
street, and has long been popular among
a large circle of acquaintances.

ACCUSATION OF
CONSPIRACY NOT
WELL FOUNDED
The Bank Freed From
Blame.
RIGHTS OF ALL CREDITORS
JUDGE DE HAVEN'S OPINION IN
INSOLVENCY.
i The Affairs of Francis, Valentine &
Co. in Involuntary Bankruptcy
Finally Disposed Of by the
Federal Court.
United States District Judge de Haven
rendered a decision yesterday in the mat
ter of the Insolvency of Francis. Valen
tine & <'â¢!. Some months ago Blake, Mof
fitt & Towne and other creditors filed a
petition in the United States District
Court to throw Francis, Valentine & Co.
into involuntary insolvency. The petition
alleged that the respondents had com
mitted an act of insolvency in allowing
themselves to be attached by the Dono
hoe-Kelly Banking Company and that the
bank and the respondents had engaged in
a conspiracy to have the bank enjoy a
preference over th*- rest of the creditors.
Judge de Haven decided that the allega
tions in tie- complaint were true with the
exception of that which charged conspir
acy on the part of th*- bank against the
other creditors; but tie- court found that
the action was commenced and the at
tachment levied upon the property of the
Francis-Valentine Company for the pur
pose of securing judgment for the amount
due from the Francis- Valentine Company
to the bank for the satisfaction of such
judgment out of the property attached,
and at all times mentioned in the com
plaint the said Francis- Valentine Com
pany was insolvent and in contemplation
of bankruptcy, and that the Donohoe-
Kelley Banking Company had knowledge
thereof and knew that if it succeeded in
obtaining a judgment it would thereby
obtain preference over the other creditors
of Francis, Valentine & Co. The restrain
ing order was therefore dissolved and
Trustee William D. Scurlock was given
full power to close up the affairs of the
estate.
j The case has been all the more interest
! ing because of the fact that the alleged
act of Insolvency was committed just
before the Federal bankruptcy law went
into effect. Tin remaining creditors tiled
the petition in the United States District
Court among the first, and at one time
there threatened to be a contest on the
matter of Jurisdiction; but the United
States District Court took hold of the
affair and restrained Sheriff Whelan from
interfering with tin- trustee in disposing
of the property of the insolvent firm.
With this decision the suit has been
: practically wound up.
HELD A NIGHT SESSION.
Judge Groezinger Listens to testi
mony in the Wong Tang Per
jury Case. "
Judge Groezinger held a night session
last evening with a view to disposing of
the case of Wong Tang, charged with
perjury, but was unable to finish the case
owing to the lengthy examination of wit
nesses and the number subpenaed.
' Wong Tang was arrested at the Instiga
tion of Ong Foon. a member of the Chi
nese Educational Society, who was ac
quitted of murder by a jury In Santa
Rosa a month ago. <ni*i Foon, the com
plaining witness in the Police Court pro
,.. dings, claims that Wong Tang commit
ted perjury on the trial of himself for al
leged extortion a few months ago. The
case has been hanging lire owing to the
absence of ting Foon in Santa Rosa. On
his return to this city he took the matter
up. as Wong Tang had testified against
him at the murder trial.
Wong Tang was placed on the stand last
night and examined at length by his at
torney, Arthur E. Mack. The other wit
nesses called were George Downey and
Gut Finn, two Chinatown watchmen. The
case will go on again to-morrow at noon.

REORGANIZATION SALE IS ON.
The Immense Establishment of J. J. :
O'Brien & Co. Is Thronged With
Intending Purchasers.
The reorganization sale of J. J. O'Rrien
<Sr Co. commenced yesterday and proved
a success beyond all expectations. The
mammoth store was thronged through
out the day and all departments were lib
j erally patronized.
The ladles in particular seemed to ap
preciate the value of th.- bargains offered
them, and many were heard expressing
their Intention to return and patronize
the establishment more extensively. The
sales were so large In volume that many
packages could not be delivered last
night, the capacity of the many delivery
wagons not being equal to the occasion.
The Immense stock of the establish
ment has been shown to the best advan
tage, so that Intending purchasers In
quest of bargains have no difficulty in
finding articles to suit their fancy, no
matter in what direction may run their
tastes.

OLYMPIC CLUB IS TO
HAVE BAR AND GRILL
DIRECTORS DECIDE THE VEXED
QUESTION.
_ .
Club Is Divided, Some Eager for the
New Accommodation, Others
Oppose It.
-.7
After several months of deliberation the '
directors of the Olympic Club have at
last decided to add a bar and grillroom to !
the club's accommodations. This propo
sition has been under consideration for
some time, as the pinion of the club was
divided m regard to the introduction of
a bar.
There are still two well-defined parties
In th.- club, sunn- who advocate the Intro
auction of the bar and others who violent
ly oppose ii. One side claims that alco
hol should not be used Internally in an
athletic club because it is likely to prove
a peril among the younger members. They
fear that the bar privileges will be
abused and that the morale of the club
will be destroyed.
As yet arrangements for th.- new bar
flnd grill have not been perfected. It is
very likely that the privilege will be given
t< John Tate, the present steward of the
University Club. He is a man of large ,-â¢<
perience as a caterer und has applied for
th.- privilege at the Olympic Club

MONEY IN CIRCULATION.
The money in circulation in the United States
has more than doubled In the last twenty years,
it has Increased 50 per cent since l.Sfi, and more
than 25 per cent since July 1. 1*36. A tabie
just prepared by the Treasury' Bureau of
Statistics shows that on July 1. 1573. the
amount in circulation was 8515.631.793; on July j
1. ISS3, j1. 373.364, 770. and on April 1, 1*33,
$1,927,816,942. No period ln mir history has I
shown a more rapid growth In the money in
Circulation than the last three years. On July
1. 1596, the beginning of the new flscal year.
the amount in circulation was $1,509,725,200. By
July 1. 1*37. it had reached $1.646.02:., 246, an In
crease of $136,303,046. On July _, 1898, it was
$1,843,435,749, an increase for that year of $197,
--407.503. and at the beginning of last month it
was $L 927,547,942, an Increase in the nine
months of the present tiscal year of $84,411,
--133. The increase since July 1. 1896, has been
at the rate of nearly half a million liars for j
each business day, and luring the past year j
has averaged considerably more than half a
million dollars for each business lay. The per
capita circulation on April I. ' ... was the
largest ever shown at that period of the year
in the history of our country. At that date
11 was, according to the official statement o_
the Treasury Department, $27 45 per capita, j
while that Of April 1. 1898. was $23 63; April 1. ,
U. 57. $23 01. and April 1. 1596. $21 53.
DRIED FRTIT IN NEW YORK.
The New York Commercial says: "Prunes re- I
main steady at about previous range of quota- ,
tions. with trade conflned to comparatively i
small orders from interior distributors. There
have been some offerings of renovated stock. I
which has Injured the sale of the better grades
somewhat; but it is understood that th" light
stocks held by dealers will exert little inlluence
ultimately. It is not the season for heavy de- |
n.and. and trade is limited to small quantities.
Prices are generally firm, though It was said
yesterday that some lots were sold at a slight
reduction from published figures.
â¢â¢Raisins are virtually unchanged. There I
have been sales recently of some hundreds of j
cases to seeders slightly below the market j
price for ungraded goods, but It was said yes- )
terday that no more were obtainable at the re- i
duced figure. Graded stocks are quiet at about j
previously reported prices. Demand is com- |
parattvely light for all grade?, and the situa- i
tion shows no indication of immediate Improve- j
ment.
"Peaches -ire in the same position as at last
reports. In general, conditions do not change, j
Demand Is restricted hy high prices and
movement is limited to the few small lots in- i
terior distributors take. Prices range at 23
@28c for peeled, with occasional sales up to 30c, |
nnd the tendency is still upward.
"Apricots are in very slight supply, and j
dealings are small. Holders nre not urging
sales, and hich prices nit purchases to actual ;
requirements of consumption." ;
THE LUMBER TRADE.
Paclflc Coast Wood and Iron says of lumber: :
â¢'The lumber trade circles of the Pacific coast
were never in a more prosperous condition than
at the present writing. California. Oregon and
Washington are a unit In the good ding \
â¢which prevails, while Arizona mills
are rushed with orders. Prices have
advanced and stand firm ' at pree
ent quotations, with a strong tendency to ad
vance. This Is In the wholesale markets, while â¢
the retail circles also feel the renewed stimu
lus and prices In their lines have likewise taken
a Jump to the good.
"The mills are running full time and are
crowded with orders, which will necessitate an
unusual activity for some time to come Shin
gle mills are being erected all over California \
and additions to the old mill plants are gen- |
erally being made so that the Ingle output i
can bf Increased. Prices stand it $1 37. but
there seems to be every prospect of an advance
for both plain and fancy grades.
"Building operations in both urban and sub
urban districts have renewed the healthy de- |
manda icr hardwood lumber, and the market I
is healthy along all lines. The quasl-associa- j
Hon still holds its prices In hardwood for cer- |
tain purposes an*l an excellent season is upon i
ar.d before the iocal hardwood Interests.
"Foreign shipments of lumber are numerous
nnd all of the cargoes are large and hold at
good rates. England has renewed her previous
demands, while the Orient. Australia and South
America, together with the Hawaiian Islands, I
fire sending in a steady stream of orders. The I
latter port of receipt has about all the lumber j
lt can utilize for some few mont I to come. I
Advices from the Orient and Vladivostok point ]
toward a good market for Pacific coast woods, I
ami the prices are responsive.
"In the Eastern States the spring trade is :
commencing and the general commercial activ
ity promisee a good year for the lumber trade, j
"The receipts of lumber at San Francisco
were larger in April than in the previous
month, save those from Mendocino County. The
latter show a decrease of 703,033 feet, while i
pine, spruce and fir advanced the figures by j
some 534,000 feet, and both Humboldt and Del '
Norte counties total more than in March.
Taken all In all. the lumber prospects are very
promising and. with advancing prices and en
larging Eastern and foreign calls. a brilliant !
year should be recorded In the history of the
lumber trade of the Ra. ;r)e slope and the West !
ln general."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather Is generally cloudy over Wash
ington. Oregon and Idaho and clear over the
remaining portions of the Pacific slope. Light
_*now has fallen over Utah and Idaho, heavy
snow in Northern Montana and rain in North
Dakota.
The pressure changes have been slight along
the immediate Paciflc Coast, but a rapid rise
has taken place over the Rooky Mountain re
gion. An area of high pressure accompanied
by unusually low temperatures .s moving south
ward over Montana.
The temperature has risen over the Pacific
slope except in Arizona, where it has fallen
slightly. â¢ ; â 'â :
Conditions are favorable for fair warmer
w^a'her in California Wednesday.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW Y.ORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, May -Stock prices ranged
over a wide radius to-day, and ended
wlth good gains for the day. A considerable j
advance, ranging from 1 to 3 points, in the !
specialties occurred in the first half hour, aided
by purchases of round amounts of the interna
tional stocks for London account. Stock values
then suffered on a continuance of yesterday's
heavy liquidation, and receded sharply, the
specialties losing from 2to nearly 6 points. By
noon liquidation had practically ceased, and
there were rallies, which, among the special- '
ties, ranged from 8* to $%. The standard rail
roads moved less widely except for Burlington
and Delaware and Hudson, which made net ,
gains of l* and 3%, respectively. Among the I
specialties, the net gains extend to 14% In Air :
Brake, 6* in Metropolitan. 3% in Manhattan,
3% ln Brooklyn Transit and an average of 2
points in Federal Steel.. Sugar, National Steel
and Tennessee Coal and Iron. Coll money j
rates ruled at 5 per cent most of the day. and ! !
their steadiness was a source of confidence as
was the easier tone In sterling exchange, which j
was attributed to the London purchase â¢of. '
stocks. Another reason which might be as- M
signed for the rally was the fact that the Ford
franchise tax bill would not figure ln the tax ;
levy of this year, and that the hope of those I
interested in the securities affected was that
the delay of a year might bring about a ;
modification of its provisions. The sharp
speculative contest between the bulls and bears
In Brooklyn Transit, which ultimately resulted
In favor of the former, was. because of it.- I
great activity, a sentimental influence In favor ! '
of taking the long side in other properties. The
dissemination ot a story that the susar in
siders were indisposed to come to terms with
present rivals because of the fear that other
syndicates stood ready to start up a new
opposition, was supposed to have something to
do with the dip in sugar, but the readiness of
a large house to absorb offerings brought about
a rally, in which the earlier loss was more than
recovered.
Besides the tax franchise bill, the rate war
induced liquidation in Consolidated Gas, but
covering of shorts and other purchases caused
the stocks to rise above last night's figures.
The rapid fluctuations with alternate pro
nounced weakness and strength caused a re
duction In the outside interest and left the mar
ket much of the day in the hands of the pro
fessionals. Late in the afternoon, when the
market broadened out and a general move
ment was under way, activity of commission
houses was again noted.
The market left Off strong at generally the
best prices. Some recessions occurred in tho
bond market in sympathy with th.- early
llquldatlon in stocks, but there were later
partial recoveries. The total rales, $2,525,001
United States 2s coupons and the new 4s
coupons declined % in the hid price.
The total sale of stocks to-day amounted to
692, 55S shares. Including American Steel and

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, May 2. â Scattered showers
throughout the winter wheat country where
fears of drought had been entertained caused
a bearish sentiment in wheat at the start. Li
quidation was heavy, and with the exception
of a feeble rally on reports of heavy export
sales, the market declined throughout the ses
sion. Further deliveries on May contracts also i
had a weakening influence. July opened %c
lower at 73* c, declined to 72% c, recovered to
73% c, declined slowly to 72% c, advanced to I
73c, and closed at 72* c. The world's visible |
supply decreased 3,531.000 bushels, against 6,
--000,000 bushels decrease for the same week a j
year ago.
Promised rains and reports of Increased
acreage weakened corn. The visible supply de
creased 2,643,000 bushels. July opened a shade
lower at J4*Â®34*c, closed with buyers at 34 V.

CALIFORNIA PRUNES IN THE EAST.
NEW YORK, May 2.â The Journal of Com
merce says: Reports of the heavy drop of'
prunes usually expected at this time are be- :
ginning to come in. Letters received from all I
the growing districts are of the same purport.
The trade here is generally disposed to accept
these reports with a good deal of allowance,
for the natural desire of growers to start the
season with promulgation of such features as
will be mi st advantageous to them, but there
are a number of prominent handlers here who,
while admitting that conditions now governing .
growing crops may not be so bad as has been
. published through the letters of Interested
growers, are of opinion that there Is go.
ground for believing the crop will turn out to
be be considerably less than last year. One
of these merchants to-day said he had little
doubt that the reported damage to the crop
was not overestimated by his California cor
respondents. He could not agree with some
of them that the present drop was due to the j
recent warm weather, but was disposed to at- j
tribute it to a loss of vitality in trees caused i
by the prolonged drought last winter, which
killed many trees and undoubtedly had a
weakening effect upon tne rest.
A feature contributing to the strong feeling
among handlers Of prunes here is the prospect
of a large demand from Eu ope this year, __
the result of reported short prune crops there.

LONDON WOOL MARKET.
LONDON. May The wool auction sales
Opened to-day with a large attendance of buy
ers. The offerings numbered 11,323 bales, in
cluding a fine show of Queenslands and New
South Wales merinos, principally greasy. The
Continental buying was eager to secure these
goods and entered Into strong competition with
the home trade, which stimulated the advance
which was most pronounced. Fine well grown
; and a small supply of scoured sold briskly.
I Crossbreds were well represented, with liner
; grades showing an advance of 10 per cent, and
mediums selling 7% per cent lower. The offer
. ings of this stock were largely taken by York
i shire. A small selection of Cape of Good Hone
[ and Natal sold quickly at 10 to 15 per cent ad
vance. The trade was well represented, except
American. The bidding throughout the sale
was very active.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Trading in local securities was fairly active
on the morning session. Hawaiian Commercial
continued to soar, touching $128.' Oceanic ad
vanced to $84^. Contra Costa Water to $71^
and Paauhau to $42*4, the other stocks remain
ing about the same.
On the afternoon call the only noteworthy
change was an advance in Gas & Electric to
$71%.
Mining stocks were dull and not materially
changed. The telegram from the pump said:
"Pump working steadily. Water in the 1730
--foot level winze is 3_ feet 11 Inches below the
station."
Charles H. Fish has resigned from the presi
dency of the Andes Mining Company, and Cap
tain Nat T. Messer has been appointed to suc
ceed him.
The sales on regular call at the San Francisco
Stock Exchange for the year to date amount
to 374,655 shares, against 678,040 shares for the
tame time in 1695.
The Alaska Packers' Association will pay
dividend No. 65 of 75c on the 12th.
The mines of Eureka district, Nev., made
the following shipments of ore by rail for the
week ending April 29th: Diamond mine. 116,650
pounds; Eureka Consolidated, 70,470, Hamburg,
40.790; Jackson, 92.160; Richmond. 132,730.
The Andes Mining Company has levied an
assessment of 5 cents per share, delinquent
June 7th.
The Seg. Belcher Consolidated Mining Com
pany has levied an assessment of 5 cents per
share, delinquent .June sth.

CALIFORNIA.
A bell buoy, painted red and marked "M" In
white, has been placed in 20., fathoms of wa
ter about three-eights of a mile off MUSSEL
POINT, MONTEREY BAY, CALIFORNIA. on
the following bearings, viz.:
Tang. Point Pinos. W. % N.
Mussel Point, S.W. hi S.. distant about Â«Â»
mile. "-vf.
End Monterey Wharf, S.S.E. hi E., about
IV; miles.
This buoy Is to be left to the southward.
This t.otice affects the "List of Reasons and
Buoys, Pacific Coast. 1898," page 15.
POINT PINOS. CALIFORNIA.
Notice Is hereby given that the Whistling
Buoy off POINT PINOS. CALIFORNIA, has
gone adrift. It will be replaced as soon as
practicable. â _ â " y-

Buoys. Pacific Coast. 1898," page 15.
By order of the Light-House Board.
U. SEBREE.

Inspector Twelfth Light-House District.
NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office located In the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense. . ' 7 V 7 V
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
offlce where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare Island, < al .â ntlM
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error. I any. is published
in the morning papers the 'ol^wlng^tay^
Lieutenant, U.S. N., in charge.

NOTEâ In the shove exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides â ,!" the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column rives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are ..'it
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
' given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign ( â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

MAJOR BEN C. TRUMAN IS AP
POINTED A COMMISSIONER.
â¦
When the Governor Reaches Sacra
mento He Will be Ready to
Name the Board of
Health.
Governor Gape arrive., from Los An
gr-tcs on the Owl yesterday morning and
went to the Palace Hotel. He will remain
In San Francisco until to-morrow even
ing and then go to Sacra- He was
engaged yesterday with ex-Senator
Stephen M. White, Judge Ross and others
in considering business matters relating
to mining affairs.
Many of the place-seekers were anxious
to see the Governor and ascertain if ap
pointments were soon to be announced.
The aspirants for the oilice of Labor Com
missioner are the most active, but the In
terest manifested In the impending
changes in the Hoard of Health is very
great. The Governor Is not paying much
attention to the contest lor presidency of
tho State University. He said last night
that he did not suppose that he would at
tend the meeting of the committee ot Re
gents to-day. He doubted whether lie
would attend the meeting of the board
next week or ever preside at any or the
sessions.
The Governor said without hesitation
and with a knowledge that the statement
would be published that he had radi
cally appointed Major Ben C. Truman as
one* of the Commissioners of California
to the Paris Exposition: He also re
marked that he had tendered a similar
appointment to M. H. de Young, but that
the latter declined to accept the position.
He was asked if Mark L. McDonald was
to be one of the Commissioners, but the
inquiry did not elicit an affirmative or
negative respense.
The accomplishments of Major Truman
were dwelt upon with delight. The major
reported the iirst speech that Governor
Cage made in California. The event oc
curred some twenty years ago at Wil
mington, and according to the Governor's
account of the affair the major not only
fully but accurately reported the speech.
Aside from this the major has had other
Training to tU him for the position of
Commissioner to represent California at
Paris. He was once secretary of the Yo
semite Commission and chief writer of the
Southern Pacific Literary Bureau. Later
he was prominently identified with the
horticultural department of tie World's
Fair at Chicago, and last but not least
wrote a book on dueling. While he was
busily engaged in preparing the horticul
tural exhibit of the Chicago fair hi was
challenged to fight a duel by a sub-com
missioner or attache of the exposition.
Th.c difficulty was adjusted without the
loss of blood. The Governor's judgment
that the major is a good man for the
place is seconded by many others, some
of whom fancy also that the place is a
good one for the major. Colonel Henry
Kowalsky is a candidate for the office of
Commissioner. He would be. delighted to
accept the place which Mr. de Young
declined.
The Governor may devote same time to
day to the. consideration of appointments
for the Board of Health. Labor Commis
sioner, the Code Commission, Commis
sioner of the Bureau of Highways and
Registrar of Voters for San Francisco.
He Indicated last night that he would not
announce his selections until he reached
Sacramento. It is almost a certainty that
some of the many oilice seekers will be
relieved of anxiety before next Saturday
night.
Governor Gage was so busy yesterday
that he did not get time to go down to
the steamship office at the foot of Market
street and pay his respects to Hon. George
C. Perkins, the United States Senator
from California.

General Eagan Here.
Former Commissary General Charles p.
Eagan, who was recently tried by court
martial and placed on the retired list ar
rived last evening from Washington, D
C, and registered at the Palace. General
Kagan was in this city about a month
ago and was recalled by the court of In
quiry to give further evidence In the em
balmed beef scandal. He is on his way to
Honolulu to visit his son, who has large
coffee plantations on the islands. Genera]
Eagan's wife is now at Honolulu, and
their stay will be a protracted one.
POSTUM CEREAL.
LED INTO IT
By Following, the Habit of Friends.
As I took my coffee in very moderate
Quantities, 1 really did not believe what
my friends frequently told me, that
coffee was the cause of the gas on my
Ftomach, which has troubled me for,
oh, perhaps forty years.
I am now a little over sixty. years
old, and I know that this gas trouble
came on about the time I was twenty,
and has been with me ever since. I
have denied myself almost every kind
of food pleasant to my taste, but with-
out any appreciable benefit. N
All of my friends have known of 'my
trouble, M it has been of such long
duration. me time ago I was called
to. nurse a sick friend who had Postum
Food Coffee In the pantry and used it
In place of coffee, praising it very
highly. For about a week I used it as
they did, as my only beverage, and the
second day I noticed a change in my
condition, and from that time on I
never had one attack of the belching
of gas. The friends, exclaimed, "Why,
where has your .trouble gone?" and
every one in the house knew that I had
been entirely cured by leaving off cof-
fee and taking Poetum Cereal instead.
You can depend upon it that never
since that time has coffee passed my
lips, and never will while I can get the
food drink, Postum. My stomach has
grown so strong since that time that I
can eat anything I like and now take
great pleasure in my meals and in life
altogether. I send you a list of friends
whom I know have been greatly helped
by leaving off the use of ordinary cof-
fee and using Postum Food Coffee in
its place. Some of these made very
flat, poor Postum, on the start as they
only boiled it a few minutes, but it la
generally known now about here that
the only way to get the flavor is to boil
it long enough. L. M. Graham, 729
Eighth Btreet, Oakland, CaJL.
THE LEELANAW IN HER NEW DRESS.
WHEN the transport Leelanaw was carrying gold-hunters to the Klondike she wore a black dress, but
now that she is under charter to carry mutes and munitions of war to Manila for the Government, '
her owners have painted her white. She was moved from Main street to the Government wharf a
few days since and it is expected she will leave for the Philippines to-day.

COL. EGBERT'S
REMAINS SENT
TO WASHINGTON
Military Escort From
Steamer to Ferry.
LEELANAW READY TO SAIL
MOVEMENTS OF THE GOVERN
MENT'S TRANSPORTS.
Captain C. B. Johnson Appointed by
the Pilot Commissioners to Suc
ceed Pilot yon Schmidt,
Deceased.
The funeral of Colonel TT. G. Egbert,
who was killed In battle, took place from
the transport Sherman yesterday. Troop
M of the Fourth Cavalry and the mount
ed band came In from the Presidio to
serve as an escort to the remains of the
dead soldier, while Major and Judge Ad
vocate Groesbeck. and Major Fie and
Captains Plummer and Noble, aids-de
camp to General Shafter were the pall
bearers. The coffin was mounted upon a
gun carriage, both being draped with the
stars and stripes and the colors of the
regiment. From Folsom-street wharf the
cortege moved to the overland ferry,
where the 4 p. m. boat was met. The re
mains will be taken to Arlington Ceme
tery, Washington, D. C, where the inter
ment will take place.
The freight transport Leelanaw sails for
Manila to-day. When she is on the road
there will be a lull, as it will probably be
three weeks before another vessel gets
away. The Grant is in quarantine and
will not be .released until Monday next.
The Sherman goes on Hunters Point dry
dock to-day, and when she comes off it
will take sixteen days to overhaul her
machinery. The Centennial arrived yes
terday, thirty-five days from Manila and
twenty-three days from Nagasaki, but as
a case of smallpox developed during the
voyage she had to Join the Grant at An
gel Island.
The return of the Centennial to Manila
is doubtful. She Is used as a cargo boat,
and as the Snerman and Grant will carry
6000 tons of freight and 3500 soldiers there
will be nothing left for her to do for a
time, at any rate.
The Grant and Sherman will require -a
considerable amount of overhauling be
fore they can go to sea again. They went
from New York to the Philippines via the
Red Sea, and after doing considerable
transport duty among the islands came on
to San Francisco. In consequence a lot
of temporary repairs had to be made at
sea, and these will now be overhauled
and made permanent.
The Leelanaw takes away to-day a lot
of muli . and horses for the army trans
port corps and the guns and ammunition
of the Light Battery that left here on the
Newport last month. A supply of feed
for the animals and a small quantity of
army rations are also aboard. Every pre
caution has been taken for the safety of
the animals. The ship is Illuminated
throughout with electri* and a con
densing engine will supply all the water
required. At Honolulu the mules will be
landed and given a rest, after which the
voyage to Manila will continue. The ship
Tacoma, now out ten days from the isl
ands, will probably get here the latter
pari of the month. She is fitted out to
carry animals ami will probably go back
to Manila with a load of mules as soon
as she can be got ready.
The Valencia left Nagasaki on April 26
and the Portland Is seventeen days out,
bo both these transports should he"in and
ready to sail again (if Uncle Sam wants
them) by tho time tho Sherman and
Grant are ready for sea.
The transport Charles Nelson arrived at
Manila in time for the soldiers to take
part in the Dewey day celebration. The
chances are that she will be coppered at
Cavite and. used as an Inter-Island trans
port. '"7 f--;' 'â â â 'â â  â¢"â  "â¢-'*'
The Pilot Commissioners yesterday ap
pointed Captain C. B. Johnson a, pilot to
succeed Captain yon Schmidt, deceased.
Johnson is a brother of the pilot who
wrecked the Mail Company's steamer
New York, .and has not been to sea for
ten years. He came out here as master
of a sailing vessel and then went Into the
employ of the Pacific Coast Steamship
Company. He was with that concern for
a very long time and brought the Santa
Rosa out here from the East. When the
southern boom broke out Johnson relin
quished the command of the Santa Rosa
and went to San Luis Obispo, where ho
superintended the building of the Hotel
Ramona. He ran that concern a while
and then came back to San Francisco,
where ho engaged in the boiler-covering
business. ,
Harry Bennett fell into the hold of the
steam collier Progreso yesterday and
broke his nose and right leg, and E. J.
Brlce was nearly asphyxiated at the Cali
fornia House yesterday afternoon. Both
men were treated at the Harbor Hospital
and will recover.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, "WEDNESDAY, MAY .3, 1899.
A POLICEMAN
USES HIS CLUB
ON A BOATMAN
Wounded on the Face,
Scalp and Lip. â¢
ATTACKS WERE UNPROVOKED
CHARGES PREFERRED BEFORE
THE POLICE COMMISSIONERS.
Officer Frank Greenan Gets Back at
the Boatman by Having Him
Arrested for Bat
tery.
When the Police Commissioners meet
to-night they will hear charges preferred
by John Barry, a boatman on the water
front, against Policeman Frank Greenan
for unofflcerllke conduct In brutally strik
ing him on the head and face with his
club without the slightest provocation,
which is a too common practice with
some officers.
According to Barry's statement, he was
in a saloon on Folsom street a week ago
yesterday afternoon drinking a glass of
beer when Greenan came in, and, draw
ing his club, made a rush at him, club
bing him on the head. Barry ran out of
the bar into a room in the rear and took
refuge under a bed. Greenan pursued
him, and, dragging him from under the
bed, kicked and clubbed him again.
Barry got out of the place as quickly
as possible and went to a saloon at Fre
mont and Folsom streets. Greenan had
apparently followed him, as he had only
been there a few minutes when the offi
cer made his appearance. Greenan again
clubbed Barry bo bad that he had to be
taken to the Harbor. Hospital, where live
stitches were put in a lacerated wound
in his scalp, two in a wound in his temple
and six in a wound on his lip.
During the attack in the two saloons
Barry asked Greenan to arrest him If he
had any charge to prefer against him,
but this the officer declined to do, which
apparently is the most surprising feature
of the whole business.
* Barry made formal charges against
Greenan before the Commissioners after
making a complaint to Captain Spillane,
and the charges will be heard to-night.
As soon as Greenan was served with a
copy of the charges he swore to a com
plaint in Judge Mogan's court charging
Barry with battery. Barry was arrested
Monday night and the case was called In
* curt yesterday morning. By consent it
was continued till to-day, when the whole
facts will come out.
Greenan has been four times before the
Commissioners recently on different
charges, and if Barry's charges aro
proved against him the probability is
that he will be dismissed from the force.
PERSISTENT SMUGGLERS.
Steerage Steward of the Curacao De
tected With Contraband Mexi
can Cigars.
The steamer Curacao is keeping up its
reputation as a smuggler of Mexican ci
gars, or rather as an attempted smuggler,
for almost every time she arrives from
Mexico the customs Inspectors find on
board from 600 to several thousand cigars
secreted about the vessel for the purpose
of being smuggled ashore. On most of
these occasions the contraband goods
have been found stowed away in the
crew's quarters, but yesterday morning
the steerage steward, whose name was
not given, was detected in the act. He
left tho steamer at the Broadway dock
with a valise in his. hand, but was
watched by Customs Inspector Stephens,'
who asked him what was in the valise.
The steward made no reply, but dropped
the valise as though it were so much
dynamite and scampered away as fast
as ills legs would carry him. He had not
been seen again up to the time The Call
went to press this morning. The valise
was found to contain 800 Mexican cigars.
They were confiscated.

Fought in the City Hall.
Bailiff O'Connor of Judge Graham's
Court and Secretary Frank Kane of the
Pacific Coast Society for the Suppression
of Vice came to blows in the City Hall
corridor yesterday afternoon. After the
fight O'Connor nursed a lump on his chin
and Kane a split ear. The case of R. C.
Clapp, owner of houses on Bacon place,
was being tried before a jury In the court
and one of the jurors had to leave for a
few minutes and O'Connor accompanied
him. Kane thought that O'Connor was
Influencing the juror and the fight fol
lowed.

UNCERTAINTY
RULES AMONG
THE DEMOCRACY
Anxious About Cen
tral Committee.
HEENEY PROTEST A MENACE
WILL BE FELT IN THE COMING
EIGHT.
Chris Buckley Said to be in the
Saddle Ready to Do Queer
Things With the Pri
mary Law.
All good Democratic eyes are turned to
ward the meeting of the executive com
mittee of the State Central Committee
which will be held in the California Ho
tel to-morrow night. On the face of it
there is nothing of Importance to come
before the meetingâ only the appeal of
Heeney from the action of the committee
of one hundred In making itself the mu
nicipal convention of San Francisco; but
an attempt will be made to have this ap
peal the basis of the downfall of the Phe
lan body and its final exit from local
politics. The Heeney appeal has been be
fore the executive committee since the
last campaign and has dragged an uncer
tain existence through several sessions,
but the political pot is simmering for the
coming fight for city government and the
old case will be made to do duty in earn
est.
The Democratic campaign has hardly
opened. There are no definite combina
tions made and no factions In the field,
but quiet work is going on all the time,
and it is a safe prediction that before
May is well on Its way across the calen
dar there will be plenty doing in the
Democratic haunts.
Already talk of district clubs is being
beard and several of the districts are
fitted out ln this respect even this early
in the campaign. Buckley, too, is re
ported to have established headquarters
and made himself ready for something to
turn up, and the committee of one hun
dred is emerging from Its hibernation and
ls thinking of primaries under the new
law and a basketful of other things which
so far can only be guessed at.
The primaries are the main object of
the purists, however, and through them
much Is hoped to be gained. The present
plan is to put through a blanket ticket
and corral everything in sight, though
that means if the new law and the old
friends will only stand for it.
Under the new law everybody votes at
every one else's primary; delegates are
named from the districts, the precincts
or at large, just as the appointing power
sees fit, ami the committee of one hun
dred has come to the conclusion that at
large is good enough for tnem. The Idea
is to have the names of all the delegates
to the municipal convention printed upon
a slip and made ready for the willing
voter, and then to have nil the commit
tees" friends vote the slip by passing it
on the primary ticket in strict accord
ance with the law, but hardly with Dem
ocratic principles.
The delegates will not be selected to
represent any particular district; they
will be taken from anywhere there Is a
man who is willing to do the right thing
from a committee standpoint. District
lines and precinct lines will play no part
in the selection, and the proceedings of
the last election, where the banner Dem
ocratic district- received two delegates
and the banner Republican district re
ceived seventeen delegates to the Demo
cratic convention, will be repeated
The new primary law will cut the most
prominent figure in the coming campaign,
for it Is something untried and not viewed
without suspicion. The election at Stock
ton only recently called attention to the
first loophole, and there is no telling how
many more will appear before the local
politicians have had their swing at it
in Stockton it was found that one side
had no contest. Its delegates were per
fectly safe, and so the surplus vote was
used to dabble in the politics of the other
side, to the great gratification of the
bosses and the chagrin of the party med
dled with.
In pursuance of the new law, the Elec
tion Commission has issued certain orders
to govern tho coming primary and among
them is the following:
Each political party, as aforesaid, shall file
with the Hoard of Election Commissioners, on
or before July 8, 1.99, its petition authenti
cated by its chairman and secretary, setting
forth: The name of the party; that the peti
tion is authenticated by the proper officer
of the party committee; that It Is the Inten
tion of such party to hold a local convention
for the purpose of making nominations of can
didates for city and county offices to be voted
for on Tuesday, November 7, 1599, In ac
cordance with Section 1, Chapter 2, Article XI
of the "New Charter," and request a place
upon the official primary election ballot. The
petition must specify in detail the apportion
ment of delegates, whether at large, or by
Assembly districts, or by primary election pre
cincts, or by combinations thereof for the pro
posed convention. The petition must be veri
fied by the proper officers of the party, before
an officer authorized to administer oaths.
The clause allowing the apportionment
p{ delegates at large Is where Â£32 blanket
ticket finds its authority, and under that
it Is expected that the entire ticket of the
committee of one hundred will be sub
mitted to each Democratic voter regard
less of his district or his precinct, and
that in that wav the committee will lose
none of its strength by reason of having
to make fights in places where lt ls not
strong. Committee of one hundred clubs
are being formed In many districts, and
it is hoped to secure a strong organiza
tion, but there are others in the field and
lt will not be surprising if the anti-com
mittee forces should show their teeth.
T__ere>are Buckley clubs in some of the
districts and there are Central Commit
tee clubs, and there are more of all kinds
being formed; but the committee of one
hundred ls far ahead with its schemes
and its organization, and unless It gets a
setback from the action of the executive
committee of the State Central Commit
tee lt will play another big part In the
charter campaign of the Democrats.
FILIPINOS AT THE FAIR..
Arrangements Made for Many Novel
Exhibitions.
Much routine business was transacted
at the meeting of the board of trustees
of the Mechanics' Institute last night.
The privilege of publishing the daily pa
per at the Mechanics' Pavilion during the
Fair was awarded to Julius Biumenthal
for $SlO, and it was also decided to con
tinue the publishing of the Library Bul
letin and the contract for the same was
let to Upton Brothers, they being the low
est of several bidders. The board donated
300 volumes to be sent to the soldiers at
Manila, 100 to the Lick Old Ladies' Home
and 100 to the college settlement at South
Park.
A letter was read from Assistant Secre
tary of War Meiklejohn stating that the
quartermaster general .had sanctioned
the request made for the transportation
of 100 tons of freights and 100 natives from
the Philippines for exhibition at the fair.
The freight and natives will be brought
over on one of the transports, the latter
at the expense of the Mechanics' Insti-
I tute. Secretary Cummings read a number
| of letters relative to the exhibits to be
I made at the fair, which showed that there
I will be excellent ones, both from the Phil
| ippines and Honolulu. Everything ls pro
gressing nicely and the outlook for the
exhibition Is exceedingly bright.
ADVERTISEMENTS.
Sure Rupture Cure.
Wonderful Restoration Re-
ported From Boston,
Mass.
_
GEO. F. MULFORD CURED AFTER
SUFFERING EIGHTEEN
YEARS.
Despite the claims so frequently made that a
surgical operation is necessary to cure rupture,
the case of Geo. F. Mulford of Boston, Mass.,
who resides at 484 Bast 7th street, is pointed
out as a direct refutation of the surgery be-
lief. Mr. Mulford was ruptured for eighteen
years and tried everything known almost, but
failed to even derive benefit. His rupture waa
such that trusses did not hold him and for this
reason the surgery Idea was presented to him
as the last resort. A final effort was made by
the comparatively new method invented by Dr.
W. B. Rice of Adams, N. V., well known an a
rupture cure specialist, and in two weeks after
treatment began Mr. Mulford was practically
a cured man without having suffered pain.
operation, detention from work or suffering of
any kind.
GEO. F. MULFORD.
