Is the fact that It hold! any kind of a rupture

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
WAR AGAINST
ROADIE DENS
TO BE WAGED
Supervisors Take a
Decided Stand.
THE "THALIA" IS CITED
PLENTY OF EVIDENCE IS TO BE
PRODUCED.
Residents in a Rapidly Settling Dis- â 
trict Object to the Granting of
Licenses for Saloons to
Improper Persona,
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, May 2.
Next Monday morning M. Casley, in
whose name is granted the license for the
Thalia roadside house on the San Leandro
road, must appear before the Super
and show cause why his license should
not be revoked. This action was deter
mined upon by the beard this morning.
The question of the disorderly houses
along the San Leandro road has become
one of paramount Importance during the
past three years, during which time the
territory has rapidly become settled, ow
ing to the electric road. Before this time
the county road was dotted with roadside
houses, but as there were no residents
near them their character was not ex- ,
ploited. Now it is different, and the hun
dreds of respectable residents in the many
settlements along the San Leandro road
have determined on a change and have
so informed the Supervisors.
This morning Attorney Kinsell renewed
his application to have the license of the j
Thalia revoked. He declared that apart
from the character of the house the li
cense had not been properly granted, and
he also stated that this objection applied ;
to several other roadside houses. The
ordinance requires that the applicant ;
must be shown to be a man of good moral
character and a fit person to conduct the
saloon decently. This is. not shown in the
case of Casley.
Supervisor Church said that Attorney
Kinsell Is not the only person who has
complained about the Thalia. A number
of residents in that neighborhood have
told him that the place was run in a dis
reputable manner.
Supervisor Mitchell said that since the
complaint mad*, at the last meeting he
had made some inquiry as to the charac
ter of the Thalia loon and had ascer
tained that it was not what it should be.
At the last meeting it was stated that
there had been an orgie at the place and
a man severely injured. Although the
complaint had not been made after the
style of a regular protest, still Mr. Mit
chell thought the board had a right to
compel the owner of the place to appear
and explain all about the light of last
Sunday week.
An argument followed between Chair
man Talcott, Mr. Church and Mr. Mitchell
as to the jurisdiction of the board regard
ing the revocation of a license when it
knew that the law was being violated,
' even when no formal complaint was
made. District Attorney Allen said that
the board could revoke any license at any
time it saw fit when It was satisfied the
law was being broken. Mitchell wanted
to know what evidence there was in this
case to warrant such a revocation, to
which Mr. Church replied that there
â would be plenty of evidence forthcoming
at the proper time.
PACIFIC UNITARIAN
CONFERENCE OPENS
FIFTEENTH SESSION CONVENED !
BY HORACE DAVIS.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, j
908 Broadway, May 2.
The fifteenth session of the Pacific Uni
tarian Conference opened in the First
Unitarian Church in this city to-night,
Hon. Horace Davis presiding and deliver- :
ing the opening address. Rev. C. J. K.
Jones of Los Angeles preached the con- I
ference sermon.
At the close of the services to-night a <
reception was given in the church parlors, j
and Colonel John P. Irish in a fitting ad
dress welcomed the delegates and visitors |
In behalf of the church. The real work of j
the conference will begin to-morrow
morning, when a business and missionary j
session is to follow devotional services.
Reports will then be received and com- i
mittees appointed, and during the day
addresses or papers are expected from j
Rev. Stopford W. Brooke of the American \
Unitarian Association, Rev. A. J. Wells
of Ban Francisco, Miss Lucretia E. Wat
son. Rev. J. T. Sunderland, Rev. George '
R. Dodson, Chaplain Frederick C. Brown, |
U. S. N. ; Rabbi Jacob Voorsanger and
Rev. W. B. Geoghegan. On Thursday
afternoon the election of officers will be
held and the conference will conclude.
The Woman's Unitarian Conference of j
the Paciflc Coast will meet in the same :
church on Friday morning, continuing
during the day and including the annual ,
election. The nominating committee of
the Woman's Conference has prepared
and distributed the following ticket for
consideration at Friday's session:
For presidentâ Miss Elizabeth li. Easton
of San Francisco.
For first vice presidentâ Mrs. Lloyd
Baldwin of San Francisco.
For second vice presidentâ Thomas

CONTRACT FOR
DREDGING LAKE
MERRITT VOID
Big Improvements to
Cease.
A CARELESS AGREEMENT
I
MONEY MAY BE USED FOR
OTHER PURPOSES.
Contractors Have Done a Lot of Good
Work for Which They May .
Not Receive Any
Pay.
Oakland Offlce San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, May 2.
The contract awarded by the late Board
of Public Works is null and void and the
large Improvements recently commenced
are necessarily stopped. Judge Ellsworth
gave a decision this morning in the action
brought by F. W. Ruppert against Con
tra* O. P. Johnson, and declared that
1 for many reasons the contract Is mi
i valid.
In discussing the case. Judge Ellsworth
said â¢hat there are many intricate points
of law Involved in the suit, but a few of
them are all that is necessary to decide
â  it. "The city charter says," said the
! court, "that the contract must specify at
what time the work is to commence and
be finished. In this contract there are
' two provisions as to time; one states that
'. the work must be finished by June 30;
the other that the work must be com
pleted within one year from the letting
of the contracts. The Board of Public
- Works seems to have reserved to Itself
the matter of determining at what date
the work la to be finished. This is in con
travention of the charter. The contract
st.it. that the work must be finished by
June 30, if the amount of cubic yards
dredged from the lake does not exceed
55,000. There is no provision made if the
; number of cubic -yards exceed this
j amount. In my judgment the contract
â¢ fixes neither time nor amount, nor does
it say that the work has to be done ac
cording to specifications.
"In the contract there were more speci
fications than there were In the adver
tised proposals. I see no escape from
these conditions, In the proposals there
is no provision for a gate at the Twelfth
street dam. In the contract this provi
sion for a gate is a very important item.
1 will not pass upon any other questions,
for these convince me that the contract
is void, and it is so ordered."
Much disappointment is expressed by
those who secured the commencement of
the improvements around Lake Merritt.
Johnson's attorney and City Attorney
Dow have not yet decided whether to ap
peal the case or not.
The dredging ceased a week ago, as
there was a doubt regarding the pay- :
ment for work done. Tne contractor has
built an expensive dredger in the lake,
has constructed over a thousand feet of
bulkhead of heavy timbers and has
dredged some thousands of cubic yards
!of mud from the lake. He has received
| one payment of J2OOO and has earned
' about $10,000, in addition to the expense
! of probably $15,000 for building, the
dredger.
City Attorney Dow says that the money
appropriated for tne work will remain in
tact, as another contract will be let. It
â  does not follow, however, that the same
j contractor will get the work. But as the
! dredger and retaining wans are necessary
i for the completion of the work. Contrac
, tor Peterson will probably be paid some-
I how.
City Treasurer Gilpin declares that the
' annulment of the contract will place
these $55,000 at the dlsposa. of the other
â â  funds after June 30. Under the law all
'â  moneys not earned in the several funds
â  by the end of the fiscal year can be
â  transferred by the Council to any other
j fund. t-^y
MASTERS MAY BE PARDONED.
Monster Petition Presented to the
Faculty in His Behalf.
BERKELEY, May Over 1000 stu
dents of the University of California have
asked the members of the faculty to re
scind their recent action in dismissing
from college the editor of this year's
Blue and Gold. Their petition, couched in
simple, respectful language, voices the
unanimous opinion of the entire student
body. Not one of the men and women
who compose the large number of under
graduates has refused to add his or her
signature. Names have been added day
by day with a readiness which only goes
to show that the matter is one over which
the members of the university feel deeply
concerned. All the students are united In
wishing to have Masters reinstated.
President Martin Kellogg was seen in
his office in South Hall this afternoon and
presented with the request and its long
fist of signatures attached. The president
up to this time has persistently declined
to discuss the reasons for Masters' dis
missal. To-day, however, he explained to
the committee who waited upon him with
the petition that it was the tone and atti
tude which the Blue and Gold had as
sumed toward the women students of the
college that had prompted the faculty's
action. :.v; _...\,r
"There "are faults and ldlosyncracles
which our professors may have," he said,
"and which we do not pretend to excuse.
It is a question how far those may be
made a legitimate Held for the satire of
the editor of the college annual's pen.
Well-meaning fun no one would object to.
But the Blue and Gold this year has
shown affront to the women of the uni
versity which is, to gay the least, un
gentlemanlv, and it was that particularly
which we felt deserving of censure."
Whether the faculty will be favorably
disposed toward the petition offered for
their consideration must remain to be
seen. The fact that it bears the signa
tures of practically all the women stu
dents in college may have some weight in
determining its disposal.
IS NOT A SVENGALI.
Dr. Anderson Pleads Not Guilty and
Tells How He Bathed His
Accuser's Feet.
OAKLAND, May 2. -Dr.' H. L. Ander
sen, the advertising Gollcitor charged with
practicing medicine without a license, ap
peared In the Police Court to-day and
pleaded not guilty, asking for a jury trial.
His case was set for May 11. Anderson
Bays that his arrest at the instance of
W. A. Boscow is "a piece of spite work,"
and he laughs at the published story of
his having exercised a hypnotic influence
over Mrs. Boscow.
"I do not know anything about the hyp
notic science," said Anderson to-day, "and
1 would not know how to begin. It is all
nonsense. Mr. Boscow's treatment of his
wife, while my wife and I stopped at their
house was of the worst kind and when
Mrs. Boscow threatened to bring suit for
a divorce we advised her to try and get
along with him for the sake of the chil
dren. â  :
"Boscow has treated me disgracefully.
Why, I have spent hours bathing his feet
with warm water and applying other rem
edies trying to make him well and this
is the way he repays me. I have never
made it my business to practice medicine,
though I am a regularly licensed physi
cian and I never Svengalled a person in
my life." . â _ . Â«.*v.
Son Against Father.
OAKLAND, May 2.â The latest develop
ment of the existing troubles between
Thomas C. Armstrong and his wife,
which, have reached the courts in a suit
to have canceled certain deeds covering
property valued at $15,000 and which the
husband claims to have executed when
he thought he was about to die, is the
arrest of their son, Samuel P. Armstrong,
on a charge -of battering the father.
It appears that tho elder Armstrong
entered his borne at Haywards yesterday
THE SAJS FBAISrCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MAY 3, 1899.
to get something to eat, when the son
set upon him and thrashed him co se
verely that he Is now under a physician's
care. At the instance of G. W. Langan,
attorney for Thomas C. Armstrong, the
son has been bound over to keep the
peace.

business of said Company, at the building

of San Francisco, State of California; that
the object of said meeting ls to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded Indebtedness rt two million
flve hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000 00), ln
United States gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of Its steamships and their equipment, for
use In the business of this corporation, and
to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company; and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon Its steam
and Falling ships and ail other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.

PROPOSALS.
. PROPOSALS
To Print, Publish and Distribute the De-
linquent Tax List for 1898.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, April 25, 1899.
In accordance with Resolution No. 2C26
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervis- I
ors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived in open session of the Board of Su-
pervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
May 8, 1899, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for the
printing, publishing and distributing of
the delinquent tax list for the year 1898,
to contain the names of the persons and
a description of the property delinquent
and the amount of taxes and costs due
opposite each name, and a notice unless
the taxes delinquent, together with the
costs and percentage, are paid, the prop-
erty upon which said taxes are a lien
will be sold at public auction. Such
publication to be made and thoroughly i
distributed on a business day, at least
once in each week, for three successive
weeks, in a pamphlet fcrr* as a supple-
ment to a dally newspaper of general cir-
culation, published in the city and
county of San Francisco, in the
English language, and said supple-
ment must be sent and distrib-
uted on the days specified with every
number of the paper issued and distrib-
uted, by carriers, by mail or otherwise.
The flrst Issued to be so distributed on or
before the first day of June. 1899, and the
others weekly thereafter, and to desig-
nate the time and place of sale and in all
respects conform to the provisions of the
Political Code and to be done under the
direction and to the satisfaction of Ed-
ward I. Sheehan, Tax Collector of said
City and County; also, to leave at the Tax
Collector's office not less than one thou-
sand (1000) copies of each issue before any
demand on the Treasury therefor will
be approved. Within one week after the
first publication of said list fifty (50)
copies thereof, printed upon one side only
of a quality of paper which can be written
upon with ink, to be satisfactory to said
Tax Collector, to be delivered at his offlce
for use In the office. Also fifty (50) cloth-
bound copies. Interleaved, to be delivered
to the Tax Collector's office within two
weeks after the first publication.
The Tax Collector must have the privi-
lege at any and all times during the exe-
cution of the said contract of witnessing
the same and giving instructions relative
thereto, either personally or through his
chief deputy, or any deputy he may desig-
nate. . . JS ; ' â; :
All proofs must be read and submitted
for correction as follows:
Firstâ Proofs must be first read by codv
and corrected by the printer.
Second-Submitted in slips to the Tax
Collector for correction.
Thirdâ to be submitted to and
passed upon by the Tax Collector, or hl3
chief deputy, or deputy; if corrections or
alterations are made by said Tax Collec-
tor, or his chief deputy, or deputy, a cor-
rected revise must be presented to and
approved by him prior to the form being
struck off, and as the work progresses
when a form Is struck off a copy of the
same to be filed with said Tax Collector.
Payment to be made as hereinafter pro-
vided, and the bill presented to be veri-
fied by the affidavit of a proper and re-
sponsible person acceptable to the Com-
mittee on Print and Salaries of the
Board of Supervisors, said affidavit to be
; made before a notary public In and for
: the said City and County, and to set
i forth that the contract for printing, pub-
: lishing and distributing the Delinquent
â â  Tax List for the year 1898, has been fully
and faithfully executed and accomplished
j In strict conformity to and with these
; specifications.

i said City and County for the payment of
! the contract price in United States gold
' coin, when satisfactory evidence is fur-
nished the said Committee on Printing
and Salaries that the said contract has
been fully and faithfully executed and ac-
complished in conformity therewith.
A bond in the sum of rot less than ten
thousand ($10,000) dollars, and with as
many sureties as the Committee on Print-
ing and Salaries of the Board of Super-
visors may elect to require for the faith-
ful execution of said contract must be
given.
Bidders will estimate with the distinct
' understanding that the Supplemental De-
linquent Tax List, If any, shall be pub-
lished for not less than one week, and
that the price to be paid for each assess-
ment on the said Supplemental Delinquent
Tax List shall be not more than twice
the amount bid for each assessment on
the Delinquent List.

' remarks and explanatory matter will be
paid for per one thousand ems pro rata
wth the average matter in said list. Said

â â  which it is proposed to so publish such
list.
Secondâ price to be charged for
each assessment, whether of real estate,
personal property or Doll tax.
Thirdâ names of the sureties to be

herein.
Each proposal must contain or have at-
tached thereto a sample of not less than

personal property matter upon the qual-
ity of paper to be used.
The party to whom this contract is
awarded will be required, Trior to or at

hereby given that on the 17th day of April
1899, the Bakersfleld and Los Angeles Rail-
way Company filed Its application In writing
in the office of the Clerk of the Superior
Court of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, addressed to the Superior Court of said
county, making application for the voluntary
dissolution of the Bakersfleld and Los An-
geles Railway Company, which said petition
and application stated that at a meeting of
the stockholders of said company or. the 13th
day of April, 1899, called for the purpose of
passing upon the question, It was resolved to
have a voluntary dissolution of the corpora-
tion, and that such resolution Was passed by
a two-thirds vote of all the stockholders and
that all the claims and demands against the
corporation, of every kind, nature and descrip-
tion, have been fully paid and discharged
Wherefore, It was ordered by the said Su
perlor Court upon the tiling of said petition
and application that the undersigned as
Clerk of said Court, give a notice of such Â«d
plication by publication In some newspaper
published In the City and County of 4__n
Francisco, for a period of thirty-flve days
Therefore, all persons having any interest tn
the matter of said application or desiring to
oppose the granting of the same are here-
by notified to file In my office, -on or before
the 22d day of May, 1899. any and all objec-
tions which they may have to the grantine of
said petition. * . *
In testimony whereof. I have hereunto set my
hand and the official seal of said court this
the 17th day of April. 1899. m>

GAGE AND BURNS IN
A SENATORIAL TRUST
Governor Slated to Succeed Perkins
in the United States Senate.
Major McLaughlin Is Not in the Confidence of the New
Alliance â How the Secrets of the Combine
Were Divulged to U.S. Grant.
THERE is not in the minds of the
leading politicians of California
the slightest doubt that Gover
nor Gage desires to appoint D.
M. Burns United States Senator
fnr California and that he will make
the appointment if the Senate admits
to a seat Matt Quay of Pennsylvania.
Recent events convince Senator
George C. Perkins and U. S. Grant Jr.
that an alliance has been formed be
tv.o.en Governor Gage and Colonel
Burns which contemplates ihe Candi
da* y of the Governor to succeed Per
kins in the Senate four years hence.
While the supporters of U. S. Grant
nt Sacramento last winter knew that
Gage was openiy hostile to ( irant they
oid not suspect that the avowed hos
tility had a deeper significance than
the desire of the executive (o promote
the political fortunes <>f Dan Burns.
"War was not declared on all who es
poused the cause of Grant. Senator
Perkins was. however, sharply criti
cised fur appointing a man like Colonel
J. P. Jackson to the office of Collector
of the P.*rt. The point was advanced
that Jackson, the holder of a lucrative
Federal office, contributed not one dol
lar to the Republican State Central
Committee. In reply to these com
ments Colonel Jackson remarked that
he contributed to the campaign, but did
not give to the committe*. as he de
sired his contribution to go where his
confidence was reposed.
There was a fixed opinion at Sacra
mento that Perkins was favorable to
Grant as the election of the San Diego
man would satisfy the south and leave
the field clear for the election of a
north man to fill the next vacancy
in the Senate. Jn this manner Perkins
expected to smooth the way for his own
re-election.
As explained to The Tall, the alliance
of Gage and Bui for Senatorial
honors does not embrace Major Mc-
Laughlin, chairman of the Republican
Ftate Central Committee. It is asserted
that the Major has not been advised of
the deal. Senator Perkins' suspicions

President.
______^ _.. .
were not excited until he went south to
attend the Free Harbor celebration.
Some facts were then brought to his
knowledge that opened his eyes. Even
then he was not convinced, but after a
conference with IT. S. Grant, which took
place in this city one day last week, the
Senator dismiss all doubt "as to the
alliance between Gage and Burns.
The secret as to the compact by which
Gage is to succeed Perkins must have
been divulged by some one high in the
councils of the Southern Pacific. The
fact that Senator Perkins received an
invitation to the recent banquet does
not signify that he is in the confidence
of Huntington and Herrin in political
matters. It is known that U. S. Grant
has powerful friends in the East, and a
word from New York may have been
Hashed over the wire.
The assertion that Major McLaughlin
has not been taken into the confidence ,
of Gage and Burns will not create in
tense surprise. The Major Is rated as a
good man for certain lines of work
He is exceptionally good to amuse big
public men who come to California for
recreation and intellectual rest. It is
not certain that Hervey Bindley has
been taken into camp. While he is re
garded as clever on the surface, he is
rated as deficient in depth.
The Grant men throughout the State
maintain an organization, and propose
to be ready to meet any scheme to elect
Burns in extra- session of the Begila
ture. Their main hope now is that the
United States Senate -will not seat Mr.
Quay of Pennsylvania.
KILLING OF SEALS
STILL CONTINUES
POINT REYES HERD PICKED OFE
ONE AT A TlMii.
Many Half Grown Animals Attempt
to Reach the Old Rookeries
Only to Be Sent to Their
Death.
The killing of the sea lions at the Point |
Reyes rookeries is still going on merrily. I
Yesterday Deputy Al Wilson telephoned
to Secretary J. P. Babcock that he is find- j
Ing enough of these fish killers at the
point to keep him busy for a day or so,
although the herd has been scattered from
the rocks upon which they used to bask
unmolested. The fusillade of Monday
was a jar to their nerves, from which they
have not recovered, but many are anxious
to get back, and consequently they fall
| victims of the rifles when they start out
on tours to prospect their old home. In
order to make things Interesting Mr. Wil
son says that he has constructed a rock
blind at the nearest point to the "roost
ing" place, from which he blazes away at
â  all that come along that way. It is cer
tain that in tins way he will greatly re
duce the herd.
As stated in The Call yesterday, the
rookeries proper are inaccessible from
any point except the water, and there is
but little Inducement for one to approach
the seal ledges from the ocean side. On
most of the tides the currents run toward
the rookeries from the south through ai
deep channel close to the foot of th*- high
cliffs. Nearly all of the prowlers approach
their old home through this channel, and :
as they near the rocks it is their habit to j
frequently rise to the surface to watch i
for dangerous intruders and to see if any
of their kind have been successful in get
ting hack. The result is a short, sharp
report that is carried landward by the
gale- which always blows against the face
of the cliff, and the seal rolls over dead
: with a bullet through its head.
Mr. Wilson's account of recent devel
opments is that the older seals are too
i clever to poke their heads into this dan
i ger, but the half-grown animals, driven <
from the larger breeding rookeries by the
! male monarchs of the herds, are compelled
to seek some place for resting, and they
i run risks that in most cases prove fatal.
These younger fellows are just as greedy
fish killers as any, and their destruction
is of as much importance as any. So far i
it Is difficult to tell exactly the number :
of sea lions killed, hut with the 150 i
slain two months ago and what have been
shot in the present expedition it is safe
to say that it will reach 800, and the game
has not been very plentiful either. Mr.
Wilson will likely keep banging away for
a day or so longer.
AN INCORPORATED CLUB.
i The Sunnyside Hall and Improve
ment Association Organized
Under State Law.
It Is the intention of the members of
the Sunnyside Hall and Improvement As
sociation to bring their organization un
der the dignity of a corporate body. With
this object In view articles of incorpora
tion will be filed with the County Clerk
as soon as they are drawn by the at
torney of the baby organization. The sum
of $2000 is Bel down as being the working
capital of the corporation. Of this amount
$205 has already been listed by the follow
ing members, who will constitute the first
board of directors: W. A. Merrill (chair
man), Gustave Schnee. A. li. Ewell. A.
F. Schneider, H. P. Hanson, John Bar
rett. William B. Pell, Peter Quinn, John
Stammer and John A. Johnson.
(in* of the first acts of the corporation
will be to build its own hall, which had
been decided on at the first .ring held.
The place selected for this site is on Sun
nyside avenue, near Acadia street, and
but two blocks from the San Francisco
and San Mateo electric car line.
This improvement club, previous to its
incorporation, did considerable for the
progress of the Sunnyside district. It
took a leading part in the sewer agitation,
wliich has for its object the constructing
of a leading outlet from Ocean View to
the Mission Ray, following the course of
Islais Creek bed. This and bringing to the
notice of the City Hall officials the neces
. sity of a schoolhouse and portectlon
against fire in the section represented by
it have been the means of drawing offi
cial eyes to this promising suburban part
! of San Francisco.

JULIUS KLEIN and Herman Hoff
man, two of the gang of poker
players arrested during the raid
a week ago last Monday night,
appeared before Judge Conlan
yesterday and were each sentenced to
six months in the County Jail for be
ing professional beggars. Klein, who
is 21 years of age, burst into tears
when receiving his sentence and was
led from the courtroom in a lachry
mose condition. They were arrested
last Thursday morning at McAllister
and Buchanan streets by Policemen
Cullinan and Geiman, who had
watched them going from house to
house begging for money or clothes.
The money they received and the
money obtained for clothing which
they sold to secondhand dealers was
utilized In playing poker at the resorts
on Market street. They were so en
gaged when the police raided the re
sorts last week. Klein was among the

SPREADING THE
GOSPEL OF TOE
BROTHERHOOD
Mission of J. S. Ingalls
to This City.
There are a thousand acres of land up in
Washington which is peopled by the mem
bers of the Co-operative Brotherhood. They
clear the land themselves, they will run
the sawmill which will cut up the timber
they fall and they have already stab
lished a cigar factory, which is adding
materially to the wealth of the little com
munity they are rapidly forming. Mem
bers are being enlisted from all over the
country, and they have grown in number
from the beginning of February, when
the scheme was launched, until now,
when they number over 4000.
The president of the Brotherhood is now
in the cityâ S. Ingallsâ and his mission
J. S. INGALLS, President of the
Co-operative Brotherhood.
is the formation of new branches of the
order in this State. He has already ap
pointed Joseph Asbury Johnson State or
ganizer, and the first steps toward or
ganization will be taken at a meeting to
be held next Saturday night. Then the
plan will be explained to the public and
the proselyting will begin.
The method of operation Is through a
fraternal body, but this has reference
only to the social side of the plan. The
real Idea is embodied in the .colonies to be
formed whenever it Is found possible or
profitable to its members. On the thou
sand acres now in the hands of the Broth
erhood it is hoped to establish a woolen
mill. The sheep will be raised on the
ground by members of the order, the w0..l
will be carded and spun in the mill on the
farm, and the work will be done by mem
bers of the order, and It will be taken to
market in a vessel belonging to the order
and manned by members.
That Is only a start, however, but it
serves to show what the plan contem
plates. The order is divided into two
classes of membershipâ the resident and
the non-resident. The resident are those
who are on the farms and who do the
work; the non-resident are those who be
long to the order and pay a monthy due
for the purpose, and who at the end of
ten years are entitled to a place in the fac
tories of the Brotherhood and support for
themselves and their families. It is one of
the rules of the organization that when
j a member has kept himself in good stand
Hoffman was at the Palace saloon.
They will be defendants In the poker
cases when they are called for trial.
Klein was arrested last January by
Policeman Daly for begging:, and got
thirty days In Jail. The police give
him a bad character.
Judge Conlan, in sentencing the two
men yesterday, said they ought to be
ashamed of themselves. They were
both able-bodied men, but instead of
working for an honest living they pre
ferred to impose upon charitable peo
ple by begging and to spend the money
they received at the poker tables. It
was frauds like them that prevented
many charitably inclined people from
helping the really deserving poor, and
he would give them the full limit the
law allowed him. If the police had
placed a charge of vagrancy against
them, which .should have been done, he
would have given them the extra six
months each.
Both Klein and Hoffman claimed
ing for ten years the order is bound to
take care of him ami give him a chance
to earn his own living, whatever hap
pens.
For compensation the plan provides that
all the working members shall receive
time checks, the value of which will be
determined by the income of the colony.
The by-laws contemplate such an equali
zation of work and hours that one occu
pation will be as desirable as another,

NORTHERN LINES
CAPTURE COAST
BOUND FREIGHT
Local Merchants Lose
Their Fight.
REDUCED RATES OFFERED
BARS ARE DOWN TO A FLOOD
OF EASTERN COMPETITION.
Great Northern and Northern Pacific
Go Back on the Agreed Sched
ule on Less Than Car
load Lots.
The big hardware and grocery jobbers
of the East are again free to send their
men and their goods into the West to
enter into active and ruinous competition
with the wholesalers on this side of the
Rockies.
The bars that for nearly a year have
held out Eastern dealers from Paciflc
Coast territory were let down on Monday
by the Great Northern and Northern Pa
cific railroads by the adoption of a de
creased tariff on less than carload lots of
freight of mixed classifications.
In an exclusive story in The Call on
Friday last it was stated that the mer
chants of this city had been given the tip
that the two big northern roads were
about to throw down the agreement en
tered into by them last May with the
other transcontinental lines that met in
Milwaukee at that time. They pledged
themselves to a higher rate on less than
carload lots on westbound freight and by
doing so lifted the unjust burden of East
ern competition from the shoulders of
coast merchants. It was further told
that the merchants here had every reason
to believe that their information was au
thentic and that, working upon that be
lief, the different associations were hold
ing almost daily meetings to discuss
plans for holding off the threatened in
vasion.
Their agents were In constant commu
nication with the influential jobbers in
Seattle and Tacoma, and with different
correspondents in the north, and a few
days ago a concerted move was made on
] the two roads. To no avail, however. The
i big firms of the East swung, too. a great
, i power, and yesterday the reduced sched
ule went Into effect.
When the Transcontinental Traffic Bu
i reau met in Milwaukee last May it di
vided freight into five classes and on less
i than carload lots raised the rate on da
1 from $2 40 to $4; on class 2 from $2 In to
, ! $2 60: on class 3 from $2 to $2 60; on class
4 from $1 70 to $1 90, and on class 5 from
$1 60 to $165, a schedule that effectually
1 shut out the Eastern firms. â¢';,'_/'
It prevented the retailer and small Job-
I ber on the coast putting in his orders to
. the Eastern firms and the coast whole
salers of a consequence got the business
1 that was justly due them.
The schedule adopted Monday by the
1 northern roads is by no means the old
rate, but another just as bad li umps
i freight noW running under schedules of
from $170 to $3 the hundred pounds into
, a general rate of $1 60. Under it the
' Eastern dealers may mix a cargo of pis
tols or other high freight with a con
' signment of tit. plat.- or other shelf goods
and ship the stuff in tinder the one rate
, of a dollar and a half a hundred
Although the merchants, of this city
, deny that the new rate will have any local
' application or Influence, the matter was
considered of sufficient Importance to
> warrant a number of them holding a
meeting yesterday afternoon and ad
journing to the Southern Pacific offices
for consultation with Mr. Stubbs, au
thority on freight matters no matter how
intricate. There the question was talked
over pro and con, and as a result of ii
the merchants here have no fear that the
Southern Pacific or southern systems will
follow the lead of the other lines.
The question resolves itself for the pres
ent into a proposition concerning solely
the dealers in Seattle. Tacoma, Portland
and Spokane, but the business men of
those towns have stood so stanchly witn
the local merchants in their fight that the
local dealers will now stand with them
and if the combined influence carries the
weight expected it will not be long be
fore the Great Northern and Northern
Pacific will be again swung into the line
of opposition to Eastern competition.
COMPETITION FOR PULLMAN.
Wagner Sleepers to Come West on
New York Central Routings.
The cut made by President "Jim" Hill
of the Great Northern on sleeping car
rates from Missouri River points is to be
not the only competition that Mr. Pull
man and his porters are to have in the
Intermittent snooze business westward.
The Vanderbllt people, operating the New
York Central line and routing through to
this city, Los Angeles and Portland, have
announced that after the middle of the
month they will operate through sleepers
to the West.
As the New York Centra] hauls nothing
but Wagner sleepers the conclusion is
that the palacelike Wagner is to enter the
Western field on Vanderbilt routings at
least, and railroad men are looking to the
near breaking up of the cinch that the
Southern Pacific and friendly lines have
had for so long on sleeping car accommo
dations.
It has been a fact for months past that
unless an eastbound traveler was routed
over the Southern Pacific system or over
lines in which that line participated
largest in profits it has been impossible
to get at call choice sections or state
rooms on Pullman sleepers. These roads
have been permitted to chart In advance
all decent accommodations, and unless a
person car. to sleep over the wheels or
wait a tedious length for a vacancy be
was compelled to so change Ins routing
that the Southern Pacific got its division.
By the system in vogue in the Pullman
offices these roads were permitted to chart
and hold up to within forty-eight hours
of leaving time every section and state
room on eastbound trams, practically
creating a premium on accommodations
and giving them leverage on Eastern rout
ing not held by other lines.
The invasion of the Wagner will likely
nut a stop to such practices and may in
time result in a reduction in the rate
charged at present for being bunkoed out
of a night's sleep by an t^ntfpped porter.
Nickel-in-the-Slot Cases.
Eleven cases of saloon-keepers, charged
with having nickel-in-the-slot machines
on their premises, were called before act
ing Police Judge Groezinger yesterday.
The arrests were made some months ago
and the Judge suggested that the de
fendants should plead guilty and he would
inflict a nominal fine. Prosecuting Attor
ney Carpenter objected to such a propo
sition, but the Judge continued the cases
till to-morrow to enable the defendants
to come to an understanding and promise
not to use the machines again.
